RATING: 6
4 plays / 2,4,5 players

LIKE: Interesting auction decisions, pricing high to keep people from getting tiles you want. Interesting scoring patterns for each round, and it builds each round. Allows players to think and plan ahead. Plays quick.
DISLIKE: A light game, not much tension.Some scoring tile icons don't make sense and difficult to discern.  A little fiddly when placing tiles. Tile draw is random, so a player can be stuck with sub-optimum tiles.

OVERALL: Fun little game. I like pricing tiles. The game is better with more players, but not quite enough tension to be a great game. Nonetheless, it's ok for a beer & pretzels night.

**SOLD. A game that I would play, but not own.

RATING: 7
Isle of Skye certainly delivers chances to make decisions every turn. I liked the game mechanic how players have to price their offering (in blind). I also liked the dynamics of the market with the amount of money in circulation changing. This helps with keeping the pricing decisions interesting through the course of the game. And Isle of Skye feeds the hunger of tile laying fans, like me.

Why not give a higher rating than a "seven" then? Well, the biggest shortcoming is with the information overflow players are exposed to. If one player really wants to analyse the situation, there is a tad too much information available for anyone to go through all possible permutations and find out the needs of other players in a reasonable amount of time. And there isn't really an efficient way to narrow the option space either, so you basically just have to assume the brute force approach. Given the limited capacity of human brain, this reaquires more time than what is the sweetspot for a family game. Moreover, the information is scattered all over the table, so there will be a lot of craning your neck. One might also complain how the game really does not feel like being in Scotland. You could basically name the game by any given mass of land and the experience would be exactly the same. However, the aforementioned problems are not big enough to ruin the game.

In nutshell, if you love tile laying you can't go wrong with this, unless you detest (blind) bidding or have gaming buddies notorious for their Analysis Paralysis.

RATING: 6
*Rating based on one play.

Isle of Skye feels like a more interesting version of Carcasonne, but not a whole lot more.  The auction mechanism is unique, but I'm not convinced it works very well.  It felt like a player could get picked on, even unintentionally, by having all of their tiles bought out from under them.  This is especially a problem for the first player in the round.  Yes, they wind up with a lot of money, but no tiles.  The next round they could price their tiles very high to ensure they keep them, but then they lose all that money and are back to square one.  In the one game I have played, the winning player had very few tiles bought off him, while the last place player had lots of tiles bought off her, and this seemed a very large factor in the outcome of the game.

Beyond the auction mechanic, the tile placing is somewhat interesting as you have to take into account what the scoring for the current round is, but also factor in the scoring types for the remaining rounds.  In the end, though, you're primarily just trying to find the spot that scores you the most points now, which is less interesting.

The auction decision is more interesting but can be highly speculative because of the tile that each player returns to the bag.  You can try to guess which tile your opponents will return, but this is somewhat of a crapshoot.

The game could be a source of analysis paralysis, and my one play felt like it overstayed its welcome because of the time it takes players to think out their auction prices, then to choose which tile to buy, then to choose where to place it.  All of this made a game that only really has 45 minutes worth of depth take more than an hour to play.  It has fewer decisions than other similar-weight games, but those decisions have more variables and thus feel more random than something like a 7 Wonders or Roll for the Galaxy, which are two games that have a similar weight level.

In the end, I found this game not unpleasant, but nothing too exciting, either.

RATING: 7.2
From Secret Santa 2016. 

An updated take on [i]Carcassonne[/i] tile laying with an interesting bidding mechanic.  With the randomized scoring markers it feels more strategic, but it also feels short enough that luck-of-the-draw seems to play more of a role than what I'd normally like. It would be interesting to try a variant where there were 2-3 rounds before the scoring rounds began, or possibly a non-scoring round in between some of the other rounds to provide more time to really build up the clan territory.

RATING: 7.5
Not good at 2 players.

Definitely a neat game, although the random draw can suck when your tiles are the ones no one wants to buy for multiple rounds in a row, and you get starved for cash.  Doesn't seem to be a common occurrence, just a rough edge case.  Even with that, all players were reasonably competetive (scores ranged from 40 to 60)

Second play reinforced scoring strategies and how to plan ahead, but I still have no clue how to price things appropriately, so that you can get 2 tiles a turn (one bought and one not-bought from you).  Scoring seems to be not intuitive, as a couple players were completely non-competitive this time in a 5-player game (10 and 23 versus winning score of 60).

Wouldn't need to own it right now, but that could change with more plays.  Also, would gladly play again.

RATING: 6
Another recent hit that just utterly fails to do it for me.

The auction mechanic, while providing interesting choices and putting a different spin on your ordinary tile-laying game, isn't the whole game.  Price too low, you get money but not points (tiles).  Price too high, you get tiles (hopefully points) but not money.  Get a bad tile draw and you're just screwed.

What's wrong with that picture?  Well, the first two are your own fault (and your group's meta) but the last is just a kick in the teeth.  Bad tile draws - tiles that no one actually wants - are a deathknell.  You can't price them high since no one will buy them and you'll be out money on bad tiles.  You can price them low but then either they're bought and you have little to show for it or you get them and they don't actually help you.  The catch-up mechanism would work except people will pay more money to those with good tiles so they get more points (meaning those with good tiles also have a big pile of coins).  And then you're still in the back, with more money, and without any point gain to show for it.

I guess my biggest gripe, besides tile draw being very controlling, is that there tends to be positive reinforcement at work since the same goals are repeatedly scored.  It's not as if that guy with 5 boats is going to mystically lose 3 before the next scoring, allowing you to catch up.  Once you're far enough behind on a goal, you're behind and *VERY* unlikely to be in contention for it.  EVER.  So the money catch-up mechanism doesn't actually close the gap - it feels more like (unhelpful) pity.

I'd like to play this one more and see if my instincts here are wrong.  I kinda hope they are, but I fear they're not.  What am I missing with this "hit" that others see?  Or am I just a super not-fan of (some) auction games?  I don't know.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Legespiel über 6 Runden mit jede Runde anderen Wertungen. Mechanismus: Man zieht jede Runde 3 Plättchen, muss eines davon in den Beutel zurücklegen. Die beiden anderen muss man mit Geldbeträgen versehen. Reihum darf man dann 1 Plättchen eines anderen Spielers kaufen, der das an dem Plättchen angelegte Geld und den Kaufpreis in seinen Geldvorrat bekommt. Plättchen, die man gekauft hat, und solche, die nicht weggekauft worden sind (daran anliegendes Geld kommt in die Bank) muss man einbauen in sein eigenes Clangebiet. Da sind einige Entscheidungen gefragt: Welches Plättchen will ich abgeben, wieviel Geld lege ich jeweils an die beiden anderen Plättchen, was kann/will ich kaufen mit dem Ziel, sowohl die Einkommensehrung nicht zu vernachlässigen, als auch möglichst gut bei den Wertungen zu liegen. Scheint interessant zu sein.

RATING: 7
As others have commented, there is nothing very original about Isle of Skye.  The tile placement is pretty similar to Carcassonne's; you'll be extending roads and trying to enclose areas in much the same way.  The tile auctions where the drawer sets the price and then pays it themself if there are no buyers remind me of Key Harvest.  Nonetheless, these elements fit together with variable bonus scoring from game to game to make an enjoyable game that is easy to explain and plays smoothly and quickly.

The artwork is quite pleasant.  Production quality is good.  The rules are clearly written.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An enjoyable tile laying and tile pricing game with a clever mechanic wherein players price tiles with their coins, which then, must be spent on the tiles if no one else purchases the tiles at the coin prices.  Then again, it seemed to me that I really did not want my tiles to be purchased since more often than not, I really liked them for myself.

I enjoyed the pricing mechanic and varying scoring mechanic, but in our four player game, I did not care for the repeated, and progressively more pronounced catch up mechanism in the game.  

I can assuredly see why the designers included the catch up mechanism, and perhaps in a two player game, it would not feel that significant, but in a four player game, a player in last place, perhaps 5 points behind the leaders, gets a huge windfall of money in the final round or two.  It felt strangely heavy handed and clunky, as if something was fundamentally wrong in the design. such that it needed such strong remedies.  

In particular, if a game design has a runaway leader problem, how do you fix it?  Change the design? Change the rules?  Change the way VP's are awarded?  Or have a very strong and repeated catch up mechanism every round?  Hmmmm....

RATING: 7
Forget the comparisons to Carcassone, this is nothing like Carcassone.

This is a fairly simple, cut-throat tile collection game where the tiles form a tableau that can be used to score in different ways - which changes from one round to the next. It can be very hard to predict which tiles you'll end up with at the end of the buying round, so while there is definitely room for long-term goals, game play is more about rolling with the punches and making the best of whatever tiles fall your way. 

Playing competitively means carefully scrutinizing everybody else's tiles during each buying round, difficult from across the table and potentially AP-inducing.

RATING: 8.5
Carcassonne: The Auction.
Silly I know but if you've played this game you might just agree with that assessment. Each round you get a hand of tiles. You must discard one and bid money for the rest. Then you reveal and try to get those tiles that you've put money towards. problem is players can always hold money back and buy those tiles out from under you. So you have to think about what tiles you want and try to reason out what tiles your opponents may want. Plan right and you can keep your tile, get one from an opponent and even make money for future rounds. The tile placement is same as you need to get certain tiles at certain because each round there is a scoring phase. And in future rounds you will score the same thing again and again. So it is critical to get what you need to score while either making your opponents pay through the nose for what they need or juts outright deny them that tile by discarding it. So it is a bit like Carcassonne but with an auctioning mechanism that we really enjoy (an exception to rule since we normally hate auction games).

RATING: 7.7
Lovely - even if rather pasted on - theme. The bidding and scoring mechanics surprise me for how well they work. There's a lot of replayability in the game through the large number of scoring tiles, of which only four show up each game, giving rise to quite some variety in the strategies you need to employ.
The game is marred by a single aspect, which is that your own fortunes depend extremely strongly on the actions of the other players. The game has too few rounds to deal well with strong setbacks. Winning requires playing well, but you can still lose through no fault of your own, which can be frustrating.

RATING: 7
Saturday gaming 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a very good gateway/light-euro that has 3 prominent elements that come together in an interesting, polished, and fun way. (1) First, you have a tile-laying/abstract element (think carcassonne) that provides some brain-burn as players attempt to visualize how different tiles would look in their clan area. (2) Secondly, you have an interested waste/buy mechanic where players must waste one tile and set a price for the other two tiles. Pricing is tricky, as you are often attempting to protect your own tiles without wasting too much money. And what tile to waste is often a difficult decisions, as you don't want to create opportunities for your opponents (they may buy it from you). (3) Finally, the game has a very interesting end-of-round scoring. Essentially, 4-tiles are randomly chosen at the beginning of the game and they score 3x each but at different intervals. This makes a game that rewards tactical and strategic decisions. 

For competitive gamers, there seems to be 2 major criticisms. For one, the game has a strong catch-up mechanic. Secondly, I'm not convinced the valuing/auction mechanic reduces the randomness in tile-drawing. It certainly seems easier to protect tiles than to purchase the necessary tiles, as you can only purchase one and another opponent may steal it from you. But, the game has very simple rules, intuitive objectives, and decisions a bit like an onion... always another layer to think about. For a gateway game, there is a lot to like.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I confess that I don't understand the Carcassonne comparisons, for they are far removed from each other, other than the square titles you are matching sides with.

Carcassonne has points scored on a common board with unchanging rules, which are very simple except for the farmers. This probably the easiest of the three classic gateways (others being Ticket to Ride and Catan).

In Isle of Skye, there is no communal board and tiles are acquired with clever auction system. Points are scored according to variable center tiles and which round it is. This is very different and much more complicated than the former

RATING: 8
Bought after a couple of friends recommended it, and after it was awarded the Kennerspiel des Jahres 2016 (over Pandemic Legacy!).

It is a tile-laying game where everyone builds his/her own tableau, with an interesting mechanic for the assignment of tiles: each turn, each players draws 3 tiles and secretely chooses one for discarding, while setting a price for the other two. Then each player can buy one tile from an opponent. The key is setting the right prices, to keep the best tiles while also maintaining a cash flow for subsequent rounds.

The idea of having a wide range of different scoring tiles to have a variety of flavors in different games is great. And the idea of scoring different bonuses at the end of each round is also great (for giving different incentives in different phases of the game).

Isle of Skye is one of those games where you can't find anything exactly new, but everything is so well put together and it flows so smoothly...

Pandemic Legacy is a great game, and I would've voted for it as KSdJ, because it was such a great experience playing it. But I can see why the jury chose Isle of Skye instead, and I must admit that it is a very very good game, in a more traditional way.
Pelican and Pfister are on fire, and I will definitely keep an eye on them!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Initial Impression: After having played this game, I've come to the conclusion that tile-laying games just are not my thing. This game reminds me most of Carcassonnes, a game I was not particularly fond of. I seem to have problems seeing the patterns necessary to playing this game well. I did enjoy the other main mechanism, a "bidding" system involving setting prices for your tiles and allowing your opponents the opportunity to buy them from you. Too low and your opponents may end up with tiles that you wanted for a bargain. Too high and you will end up depleting your resources as you pay the price to the bank. Overall I enjoy this mechanism but will probably stick with Castles of Mad King Ludwig which includes a spacial aspect as opposed to a pattern recognition component. Still, I would like to play this game again if only to verify my ineptitude with it.

RATING: 8.5
This one just clicks for me.  I love the price-setting/purchasing phase, and I love the puzzly elements of actually laying down your tiles.  While the actual tile-placing rules are very simple, the scoring tiles you have in that particular game means placing tiles can be VERY strategic.  I love that with the 16 tiles, you get a lot of replayability, and how the purchasing phase is an interesting sort of self-balancing system.  It just intrigues me and has a nice mix of mechanics that end up working very well together.  A more thoughtful game than Alhambra, and I have found it a pleasant mix of tension in purchasing phases with relaxing tile laying.  I liked the pacing!

RATING: 8
Plays fast with 4-5 players and each playthrough can be quite different from others based on the random scoring tiles and/or the players' pricing habits. It's also very accessible to non-gamers, so its rating gets bumped a little bit for achieving both accessibility and gamer-friendly-ness.

The mechanisms provide some advantage to player who are familiar with its strategy. For example, after a few plays you start getting a feel for how much a victory point is worth and you can start pricing the tiles efficiently. Despite the advantages over a newer player, the game's built-in catch up mechanism keeps everyone involved until the end.

This is a game that is easy to explain to non-gamers; though they may need a full playthrough before they fully understand some of the tile strategies.

Haven't managed to play this with 2 players, yet.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm always a fan of games that involve tile placement, and building your own thing. While it can be frustrating trying to figure out how to price your tiles in this game, I still had fun with that mechanism. The balancing act of pricing high enough that you're not giving things away, but not so high that you have to buy it yourself is enjoyable. I thought there were a few minor flaws, though. The first of which I was assured was pretty rare for this game, but in my one play the catch-up mechanism had a massive effect particularly in the last round and therefore felt overpowered. It just seems wrong that a player can literally be only a handful of points behind the leader and suddenly get a windfall of 9 coins. The other issue I had was that it seemed there was maybe one layer of complexity too many. There were certain elements that felt unnecessary. The last thing I struggled with was getting my Carcassonne-trained mind to see the land areas in this game and to eliminate the roads as a dividing line. Perhaps it was the choice of artwork in Isle of Skye, but I never have the problem of accidentally placing a field next to a city in Carc, but here it happened more than once. Yet, despite any nitpicks, it was an enjoyable experience and both my parents thought it was a game they might try again.

RATING: 8.8
First time I played this game Aug/16, I knew how much I liked it after the second round of play.   It isn't a terribly long game, but that is to its benefit.   It is a bit of a mad scramble to gather points when ever you can in the limited number of rounds there are.    This feels like Carcasonne meets Keyflower.   

Drawing three tiles from the bag and making the decision that you want the tile sold, or wanting to keep the tile yourself is fantastic.   If you want to keep the tile because it is useful for you, you have to set it at a price that would not make it enticing to the purchasing player.   But the price you set it for, is also the price you have to pay for it.   Therefore setting it to high forces you to possibly overpay for it.  There is a simplistic complexity there that I find so intriguing. 

The scoring system in this game is also brilliant.  You draw four scoring tiles from the set of sixteen, and those four are randomly placed under A, B, C, D.   Examples of a scoring tile would be; One point for every farm animal, or five points for the most ships.    But each of the rounds played, scores a different tile, and in some cases, multiple tiles.   So planning things out is intriguing, as you can try to score points on every round, or try to build up for a big score on a given round.

This is a very satisfying light/medium, non-thematic strategy game that is very accessible to most gamers.

RATING: 8
Carcassonne meets Kingdom Builder meets Key Harvest.  It's got the tile laying of Carc (with relaxed tile placement), the variable end game goals of Kingdom Builder and the setting a price/buying/selling of Key Harvest (albeit with money not goods).  

While I love the game, I much prefer it with 3 and 4.  With 2, it feels too balanced as each player usually ends up with 2 tiles and if one round, one player gets 3, the next round the other player almost always gets 3.  With 5 players, it's just hard to keep track of everything going on and it's easier to accidentally get screwed or helped.

:thumbsup: [b]POSITIVE POINTS:[/b]

++ Great, simple, light-medium weight tile-laying game with blind auction mechanism to buy tiles, that can be surprisingly both tactical and strategic.
++ Highly variable game scoring depending on the 4 scoring cards drawn (out of 16) at the beginning of the game and also according to their order when drawn (A, B, C and D) as each round scores a combination of these cards differently. Also, some tiles have individual scoring opportunities.
++ Great replayability from the highly variable game scoring and the tiles draw order.
++ Easy to teach, fast to set-up and fast to play.


:thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]

-- Really good 2-players game, but a bit better at 3 players as this offer more choices without without lengthening the game too much.
-- Especially at higher player numbers, the auction phase and the buying phase can take a lot of time depending on the players (as these steps can be AP-prone). But this can be easily mitigated by the use of a sand timer that can be always kept in the box.
-- [-]I wished there could be an expansion to further expand the scoring tiles (there are some possibility that are not covered), and maybe even add some new twists to this game[/-] (It's coming in 2017!).

RATING: 9
A neat, light-ish game of placing tiles in order to best satisfy a selection of goals, which differ from game to game.

Each turn, every player gets 3 tiles, setting prices on 2 of them, and discarding the 3rd. Then in turn order, everyone gets the chance to buy a tile from another player at the set price. If, after everyone gets a turn, you have any tiles that didn't get bought, you must pay your set price to keep it for yourself. So, each turn, you'll get to place 0-3 tiles into your personal landscape.

Then players look at the scoring for the round. At the beginning of the game, 4 different score tiles are randomly chosen which dictate the scoring for the game, so you're goal will be to maximize the points you get in each category. Additionally not every scoring tile is scored every round, so you'll need to get the timing right as well to score the most points.

Overall, a very neat game with seemingly a lot of good replay value due to all the scoring tile possibilities, combined with what tiles are drawn by the players. Good stuff!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I like that the tiles get offered to your opponents first but you set the price. If they don't buy the tiles you have to pay for it at the price you set. 

In Carcassonne you add tiles to a shared area, in IoS you are only adding to your own area. This means in IoS there is not much stealing your opponent's work (not a bad thing just a difference to Carcassonne).

It is annoying (because of OCD) that roads can abruptly end when matching terrain tiles.

RATING: 7
Fun and fast little game. Borrows Castle of Mad King Ludwig's "master builder" mechanic where you price tiles for other players to buy, with the improvement that everyone is does it simultaneously every turn so that there is less downtime. That alone is a big selling point, IMO.

The game moves at a crazy fast pace, so players are forced to choose when and how to score. The variable scoring criteria should keep things fresh (again, it worked in Ludwig!), but adding the timing element where certain objectives only score on certain rounds will really make for some nice variety. it's all very clean and straightforward, but with those tense juicy choice points.

That said, the transient scoring and fast playtime leave me with a weird dilemma: it's just hard to get emotionally invested in my island (and thus the game). There's DEFINITELY opportunity for long-term planning here (and someone who can exploit that AND be opportunistic will win), but it all feels a bit fleeting and then it's over. Still, I think I have room in my collection for a fun puzzle that winds up feeling a bit like a diversion.

The only thing I outright dislike about this game is the catch up mechanic where the player in last place gets more income. I don't even really care if it works or not, it just feels so contrived. Everyone I've explained the game to has rolled there eyes at it. It isn't something like Power Grid's turn order system - which you manipulate throughout the game taking the best and worst positions to smartly further your strategy, but just... "you're in last. have a cookie". I can deal with it, but man it reeks of inelegance in an otherwise very streamlined game.

I suspect this is best with 3 players. With two, I think the economic game loses something when there's a certainty that you'll get one of your own tiles every turn. With 4, much like Ludwig, it's extremely hard to keep track of who is doing what and thus price tiles appropriately. 

PS: I suggest using a house rule where you must take the piece you axe and taunt the player it would help the most with it as you toss it back into the bag. Improves the game tenfold.

RATING: 6
Really liked this game the first few plays, thinking it to be a replacement for Carcassonne (it is absolutely not).  This game is profoundly broken out of the box, with an almost guaranteed tendency to produce a runaway leader (essentially ruining the game every time!).  This can be remedied by randomly selecting 5-6 goal tiles and then removing the ones that are too similar to each other prior to starting the game.  The auction mechanic can be great fun, and is the heart of the game.  Most new gamers will like this game, but some experienced gamers will hate it with a passion.  Recommended for 3+ players.  The catch-up mechanic is a corny bandaid that in no way fixes the problems with this game. I knew that something was wrong when I could predict the game's winner after the first round!

RATING: 3
Disclaimer: I was having an exceedingly hard time keeping my eyes open during this game owing to illness-induced fatigue.

I seriously don't get the enthusiasm for this title. It's tile laying of course, and the roots of Carcassonne are clearly visible. The game gets kudo points for using changing scoring goals: always a nice mechanism for introducing variability in play. So far so good. But in six rounds you can't do a lot of build up, so that scoring, so attractive at first, becomes a bit ad hoc. Okay, I guess. And then there is that ridiculous auction... what's it doing here? I understand the questions you're meant to ask, but as with any game involving simultaneous hidden guesses (blind bids is appropriate to use in relation to this game) these questions must be [i]simple[/i]. They are not that in Isle of Skye (far too many variables ranging from your own tile world to that of opponents, plus scoring opportunities, plus cash situation, plus ...), and thus utterly and irreparably fail on a fundamental level. It's very difficult to steer things you want them to go, and so at the high end of the player count player chaos reigns supreme. 

It all leaves me scratching my head quite a lot. I think I'm going to have to conclude that after [i]years[/i] of Carcassonne-domination, people are just glad that it got a few new mechanisms to play with. I'm sorry, that's not going to cut it for this old grizzled gamer.

RATING: 9
[b]Miniature Market[/b]
This game may have replaced Carcassonne for me.  I doubt I'll ever get rid of Carcassonne, it was one of my gateway games, and I've invested in with a lot of expansions.  But this one gives more purpose.  There are four random scoring objectives that'll trigger at various times throughout the game.  That give you a purpose in searching for various different tiles to add to your kingdom.  Some tiles also have additional bonus scoring that'll trigger at the end of the game - which doubles if you complete (enclose) the terrain on which the bonus scroll lies.  It's a quick game, rather light, but fun.  I can understand how it won the Kennerspiel de Jahres award.

Played this game with my gaming group.  They were not impressed with it.  But I still enjoy it, so there! :P

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I'd heard this was a bit like Carcassonne, but a step beyond, and the description is accurate. There are two nice twists that make this game stand out.

First, you have a secret pricing system where players are setting a cost for their tiles. The key is to keep prices low enough when you want players to snatch them up, and high enough to scare them off when you don't. Secondly, there is a shifting in what's scored each round (which changes game to game), so timing is just as crucial as what you put down in front of you.

Anxious to try this again with various player counts - I think it has a lot of potential.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Components: Sturdy tiles and drawstring bag. Functional coins in different sizes and colors for the different denominations. The artwork is great, with lots of fun little details that reward close examination. Even more satisfying than Carcassonne to see my tile landscape build up over the course of the game.

Experience: The economy in this game is really interesting. Managing my income; controlling who I pay money to; setting pricing; trying to defend a tile from purchase; axing a tile I don't want anyone to have. I've also noticed that if I raise the price on one of my tiles, it increases the value of my other tile, because in a 2-player game it's usually better to get any tile than no tile.

I love building my kingdom, trying to keep my road options open, and to adhere to the various scoring goals in play. Like Kingdom Builder, having different combinations of scoring goals makes each game feel distinct. With the addition of individual scoring goals on the tiles themselves (and the opportunity to double them), each player begins to value tiles differently.

The goals lend themselves to really different styles of play. Some emphasize the shape of my chiefdom, some emphasize how my tiles are connected by roads, some want me to have many small completed areas or a few large completed areas. The different permutations make for a fresh game each time.

Awesome game. Fast-moving.

Variant: Played one game where each player has a private, hidden scoring goal. That was fun too.

First Played: 2016-10-17
Last Played: 2017-10-08
Plays (31):
2 players: 18
3 players: 6
4 players: 7

RATING: 7.5
[15 Aug 2017] - 7.5 (6 plays)
Reviewing this rating and bumping it up. The brilliance of this game isn't in the rules or the mechanisms but in the auction that happens. First, each player needs to set the price for two tiles and determine which one to remove from the game. Then, each player needs to determine if that price is fair and if they want to buy the tiles that are for sale. The whole player driven market makes the decisions involved deeper and richer than one would expect from the surface of a simple tile laying game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Has any designer had a better year than Alex Pfister did in 2015?

Another exceptional game. If Pfister wrote a mystery novel someone would get shot in the first paragraph of page one. He doesn't ease into anything - all his games seem to start in the middle. I love that. And although they're surprisingly heavy games for their short length, Pfister seems to know that agonizing decisions don't induce AP when you're dying to see what will happen next. 

He just gets game design. If you like Isle of Skye, don't miss Oh My Goods! and Mombasa.

edit: With 5p it was hard to feel like you had much control. In the 2p game pricing your tiles is incredibly angsty. You want to price them just high enough that your opponent can't buy while keeping enough cash in hand to pick up one of theirs.  

With 5p someone would buy one, or often both of your tiles, no matter what prices you set. Players who got bought out last turn have enough cash to pay whatever price you can set. 

IoS is still better by far than most 5p games that clock in under an hour. But it is remarkable in many ways with 2 that are diminished with 5.

RATING: 6
Boy did I get destroyed in this game.  Lapped.  But it was a pretty good game in the vein like Carcasonne.  You build your own city and score based on your tiles. RATING: 6.5

Isle of Skye is an advanced form of Carcasonne where you put a price on the tiles you randomly pick and try and sell them on to your fellow players, or price them out of the market so you can buy them yourself.  With scoring dependent on the type of tile you have, the value of the tiles will vary for each person dependent on what they are collecting.  It is clever but I think the simplicity of Carcassonne is preferable as both are ultimately tile-laying and scratch that same itch.

Sold on after a play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Isle of Skye is my current favorite tile-laying game, by a pretty decent margin. It's simple to learn the rules and to play - but is surprisingly "think-y" for a light-weight game. It's what Carcassonne should have been. The combination of the auction, the varying objectives (different in each game), and tile-laying makes it feel fresh, even though it largely uses tried and true mechanics. It's a fast game (30 mins to an hour), with good player interaction. It's fun to play, satisfying to build your kingdom, and aesthetically pleasing, as well.

That said, the game isn't perfect. It's not always going to be a "balanced" game (there are 16 objective tiles, you draw four out at the beginning of the game, and they are scored at different times throughout the game depending on the order in which they were drawn). It's also one of those games in which you feel like you have more control than you actually do - a lot of doing well in the game is getting more tiles than the other players (not necessarily better ones), and there's only so much you can do to block your own tiles from being bought. 

All in all, a definite winner. Definitely recommended if you like the concept of Carcassonne, but not the execution of the game.

RATING: 4
2016-03-17 : This game is the answer to the question of how to put more gamer elements into Carcassonne. Instead of random tiles, the tiles are auctioned. Tile placement rules are relaxed, but if you can manage to place them perfectly you can score more points, more scoring opportunities are given etc. The problem is that at its heart it remains Carcassonne and I just happen to not particularly like tile placement / tile matching games. They all try to have cute art which hinders play - form over function - making it all but impossible to quickly scan your opponent's status. I waffled between giving this a 6 or a 4, but the lousy graphics (from a playability pov) and the catch-up mechanic that essentially destroys good play means it's going to be a 4.

RATING: 9
This game is a cracking mash-up of Carcassonne and Castles of Mad King Ludwig. How so?

Carcassonne:

You are placing tiles with various scoring features next to other tiles in a way that the landscape matches (in most cases). Isle of Skye differs in that you don't have to place components on the tiles to score them and players are not placing tiles to a communal area. 

Castles of Mad King:

You use money to buy tiles. Beyond that, players need to be able to value the tiles for the round. Each player is also building their own little area. Isle of Skye differs in that each round all players set prices to tiles rather than a single player valuing all the tiles for the round. This usually leads to less downtime.

So, what else makes Isle of Skye a unique game? Well, each game you will randomly choose 4 scoring tiles out of a bunch that come with the game. Over the course of 6 rounds each of these tiles will be scored 3 times each. Even if you have the same 4 scoring tiles the game will play differently based their position on the round tracker. This game really appeals to me due to being less directly confrontational than Carcassonne, quicker than Castles of Mad King Ludwig, and extremely variable.

If you play with two, do use this variant:
https://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/1421967/2-player-low-maintenance-dummy-player

RATING: 8
Despite being one of the most accessible games in my collection; Isle of Skye is chock-full of rich decisions. The balance of pricing your tiles while leaving your wallet plump enough to buy other tiles is fabulous, and the end-round scoring mechanism forces players to develop both short-term and long-term strategies. The catch-up mechanism seems too strong on it's face, but is quite fair in practice, even at the higher player counts. Isle of Skye is a game of assessment. Determining the value you place on each tile for yourself, and weighing that against the presumed value to your opponents is the heart of the game, and makes the game highly replayable since the valuation methods (scoring tiles) change not only round to round, but game to game as well.

RATING: 4
The thrust of the game is figuring out how much of a price to demand of the tiles you offer each round. The catch is, you must put the price on your tiles with your own money (imagine someone putting a $100 bill on an item instead of a price tag). The money you put on the tiles is money you don't have available to purchase someone else's tile. You soon realize that your best bet is to price things so high that nobody buys them because then you get them yourself. 

Figuring out how much to place as a price to keep people from buying your tiles vs hold back as cash is one of the two ostensible fun points of the game. The other is taking the tiles you've won and placing them on your tableau to create whatever Carcassone-like requirement it is the turn demands of you. Carcassone on steroids is trite but rather apt. Personally, I don't find either compelling, especially for how long the turns take. 

The problem is the game is completely opaque. You don't know how much people can afford or not nor do you know if buying certain tiles will pay off down the road. Still, you spend several minutes locked in AP as you try to figure out the right bid for the right tile(s). Then you wait for your opponents to finish their computations. Then you do it again. In the end, you spent a lot of mental computation time over what amount to be guesses. The actual decisions aren't that interesting to me. It feels like some very heavy lifting for what is ultimately a rather light load.

RATING: 7
I really like the twist of the tile laying whereby you draw three, discard one and buy/sell the others, and I think it mitigates a lot of the luck of the draw that can cause a runaway leader problem in [thing=822][/thing], however there are a heck of a lot of things on these tiles, not only do you have three types of terrain you also have farms, cows, whisky, sheep, ships, lighthouses, and others, which all score in different ways, dependent upon the tiles you have previously drawn and the starting objectives. 

It is actually quite difficult to predict how in demand the tiles you just drew could be and thus difficult to set a price and this is the main meat of the game. I think more familiarisation with the game would lead to better pricing of tiles and thus allow the game to flow more, but our first place certainly felt very stop start and clunky.

I really do like the concept however and will be pushing for more games of this soon.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Even though it is not an entirely new mechanism, the pricing of tiles is what differentiates Isle of Skye from most of its tile-laying brethren. Each turn players need to set prices for the tiles they draw, and the other players get first choice of a tile to buy. Tiles that do not get chosen have to be bought by the player who set the price.

In theory, this could lead to players setting prices individually according to current circumstances and special needs of certain players. That would probably significantly increase play time, however. In practice, many of the tiles are quite similar, and since there is a steady influx of money into the game, over time all tiles generally become more expensive with (relatively) minor differences depending on the scoring conditions in this particular game. And similarly, you typically don't want other players to buy your tiles at all because in many situations more tiles is better and you can acquire at most one per turn.

RATING: 7
I've heard good things about Isle of Skye, so I was intrigued to play. Overall it has some great things going for it. Carcassonne-style tile laying is never an issue for me, so check!  And this uses the auction mechanic from 'Key Harvest' (whether intentional or not) where you set the price for your tiles with your money and either a player buys it from you (getting your money back to but losing the tile) or nobody buys it (and you gain the tile but lose the money).  I love that mechanic! Beyond that, I found the game a bit standard and not diverse enough to make it a true, innovative winner in my book. I appreciate the attempt to make the game variable with different scoring tiles, but I can't imagine the different scoring methods would really change the way the game is played all that much (as it all boils down to set collection and tile placement in the end, no matter the variables).  Also, I think the art is rather sub-standard, which is a real shame considering how signature Klemens Franz's work typically looks.  Also, this is another one of those games where you play best by analyzing everybody's shit thoroughly - and I don't exactly care for the time that takes.  And it does seem like a necessary part of the game to constantly scan everyone's tile-board and generalize how many points they can earn, etc.  Overall, a good game but probably one that will take a backseat as time goes on.

RATING: 8.5
TI rate 6 strictly for the 2 player game as that is what I've played, I expect it to get much better with more players and will revisit my rating then.I love the idea of this game, it has very simple rules but a lot of interesting, tense choices. I don't usually like tile placement games, but I think it helps that you can interfere with each other's boards and that you get the tiles you want (if you're willing to pay). I like the wide variability in scoring and that a tile could be amazing for one player and worthless for another, or amazing or worthless depending on the scoring tiles. It's fun setting the prices, keeping in mind that you need to be able to afford the other players' tiles with the money left over, but being able to push your luck and hope a player before you buys one of yours.

The game suffers at 2 players, though. As there's only 1 other player to buy from, there is plenty of opportunity to price-gouge them. It leads to a more cut-throat but less interesting pricing game.

RATING: 8
IoS is both a bit odd and a bit charming because of the dual nature of the game. The first part is the sales phase (auction is not quite right) and the second is the tile placement phase. While on the surface that seems like it wouldn't work or, at best, be disjointed, it's surprisingly smooth. I like how you've got a bit of player interaction in the sales and then a bit of navel-gazing in the tile placement. 

A couple of notes from a recent play:
-Everyone thought the game ended a round too soon. Several players mentioned that they would like one more round where all the bonuses scored.
-After playing at both 3 and 4 players, I think 3 is the ideal player count. With 4, it is too difficult to both consider your own strategy and get a sense of what other players need or guess what they will offer up.
-Still a bit confused by the scroll scoring. Need to read the rules more clearly on that.

This is still a game I would like to have in my collection but I don't absolutely need it.

RATING: 8

Edit: After a bunch of plays I've realized I'm pretty bad at this game! As in, I've only ever won twice and both times I was teaching people who were completely new. You can get locked out of the game which is no fun, but for the most part, the frustration I've felt has really just made me want to play it more. And this last time I played was one of the closest and best times yet!

Edit: After playing the game literally 9 times, I found out I'd been playing and teaching it wrong (basically I hadn't known there was a 1 buy/turn limit. This makes pricing and way more important and prevents you from getting locked out of getting any tiles on the first round which was basically an auto loss). This change is fairly minor in terms of overall game quality, but does make it a bit smoother. That being said, 10 games in, still really bad, but still can't stop wondering how it'll go next time I play. Love it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
I played this at hunter&cron BerlinCon against one of the designers and one other player. It's a beautiful game which probably reminds one of Carcassonne as the first impression. Besides the tile-laying and world/island-building the main mechanics are: bidding for tiles every round and buying them from the other players; and the scoring phase which scores 1-3 of 4 different scoring criteria every round.

All in all my first play was okay to good. Of the 3 main mechanisms I like the anticipation of the different scorings best, I guess. I'm also (usually) a fan of bidding. The problem I had was that each round you basically have to evaluate 3 tiles per player (everyone draws 3 and you can bit on yours and plan to buy other player's tiles), which in our 3p game was 9 -- which can, at least for me, trigger analysis paralysis. Even if some tiles are an obvious "no-fit", you still have a handful to take into account. Given that, I guess 3 players is more than enough -- I fear that 5 players would take the game into unpleasant territory. 

I wouldn't buy it yet, but I'm eager to try out the other scoring tiles.

UPDATE: played a second time. Down-rating from 6.9 to 5.5 — Sorry, this just isn't my cup of tea. While I can see the good design and understand why people like it, I personally have almost zero fun playing it. For a family game the tile-evaluation/bidding phase is too AP-triggering for me. I could ponder 10 minutes over this, but then again I don't want to play this game for 2 hours. This time we were 4 players, which made it worse, as I expected it after my 3p game. Maybe I'd try it *once* again as a 2p game. But i's unlikely.

RATING: 8
For me this in my very own Carcassonne. I don't own Carcassonne, but I have played it several times and it's really good game. In this one I see a lot of what makes Carcassonne so good. Tile-placing mechanic with a lot of bright colors and interesting historical theme. Similarities between these two games doesn't just end with tile-placing and theme, but there's still plenty of things to differentiate these two. Carcassonne, even with two players, is considerably longer of these two, where as Isle of Skye has some extra mechanics to consider, like auction mechanic. One negative thing about Isle of Skye is the box of the game: there's no inserts, no plastic bags, nothing to help you to keep your components in check and in good order. And just like in Carcassonne, there's a lot of components in this game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Isle of Skye is a smart mid-weight tile-laying game, which takes off from Carcassonne or Fjords, and adds some market valuation as well as some set-building mechanisms, which gives some of the flavour of Castles of Mad King Ludwig.  

Players draw income from whiskey distilleries linked to their castle by roads, then they select 3 tiles, and set pricing secretly for 2 while flagging the last for discard.

Price is set by placing one's own money on a tile- in effect, a firm offer to buy a tile.  This can be used to buy a tile (likely at a high price), or can be used to accumulate funds for other tiles. Each player gets a chance to buy one remaining tile from other players if desired, and if a tile is sold, the player gets their stake back as well as the asking price.

Players place tiles, and then, based on round, variable scoring tiles are scored for points- nice planned play variability.

At game end, each 5 money is translated to a point (Scottish thrift), and some tiles have bonus point banners which are scored at game end as well, which can double for being in a completed region.

By taking away the area control aspect thru player boards, this adds a self-building feel, as well as a level of control over how things lay out.

Players have some interaction thru the pricing mechanisms, and the ability to potentially buy away a desirable tile, if income and bank allows.

The variable turn scoring means that there are many ways to score, some competitive (most ships), and some independent (completed regions).

The artwork is lovely, the rules simple, and 6 rounds play quickly.

All in all, this is a tasty combination, and I have no hesitation in giving a strong initial rating, with a an expectation to likely increase over time with plays.

This is just a fun game, easy to play at many levels, and seems to invite one more round- a must-buy for me, and an excellent choice.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.6
起始設置:
根據玩家數使用對應面圖版分數0，隨機放四個計分板塊，每個人拿一個屏幕，起始城堡跟小木槌版塊。
*年輕的玩家起始，每輪六階段，六輪後結束，最後記分(5人5輪)
1.收入 城堡5元，每個跟城堡相連的道路上有酒桶+1元，根據回合落後補償，落後幾人*補償上金額幾元
2.每人抽三個版塊放在屏幕前，並在屏幕後面對應位置選擇兩個放錢，一個放棄置版塊
3.對應棄置版塊的丟回袋中
4.起始玩家開始輪流最多可以跟別人買一個板塊(價格為該玩家的定價)，收錢的同時把訂價的錢拿回手，另外一塊板塊就要自己付錢買
5.拼圖 邊邊一定要對應，****路不用相接***
****包起來的區域可能會有額外加分(馬路不算)，不能放的丟回袋中
6.回合結束根據圖版對應計分板塊計分，每塊會計三次分，換起始玩家
**遊戲結束版圖上的捲軸記分，如果卷軸在封閉地形分數*2，五塊錢一分

RATING: 8.5
My favorite filler of the year! A quick simultaneous auction-pricing tile-laying game that works for two or more. Simple tile laying reminiscent of [thing=822][/thing], with scoring combos for each game that add a combinatorial explosion of replay value.

Yet, the secret simultaneous selection of one tile to discard, price two that you must buy if not sold to others, coupled with the option to buy from others, just makes for a fantastically interactive game, not experienced since the likes of [thing=34084][/thing] or [thing=172][/thing]. 

Might be higher if the scoring strategy shifts from round to round were more intuitive.

Durante el juego vas a ir adquiriendo losetas de territorio para construir las tierras de tu clan. Pero lo fascinante es que cada round tiene un criterio de puntuación diferente. RATING: 5
This might just fall into the category of "not my thing". I enjoy it, but it's not something I would generally be really keen on playing.

Things I like:
- Victory conditions being random means that the values of different tiles vary wildly from game to game.
- The price setting and turn order makes for a fun rhythm to sync up your cash reserves to.

Things I don't like:
- The spatial element never really comes to the front, and there's no real way to control what tiles you are building towards, since tiles are fairly random.
- Victory conditions and tile draws can occasionally interact in weird ways. Lots of points available for brochs, but no brochs show up? Wonk wonk.
- The interaction between players is fun at three player, but it's hard to team up on someone who is in the lead when they pull bad tiles.
- In many cases, you want to have bad tiles in front of you so that your opponents don't buy them off you, giving you a lot more territory to play with. In other cases, drawing bad tiles can leave you cash starved when no one will buy your tiles. Both cases are not controllable.

Nitpicks:
- Setting prices is sweet and all, but it's pretty obvious which tiles you are putting money on, since people can see you stacking and moving things around behind your screen.
- Some victory conditions are super boring in two player games. Any of the 5 points to first, 2 to second, especially.

I've played 2x two player games, and 2x three player games. Two player seemed more interesting to me.

RATING: 6
Reseñado
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Juego muy ligero para terminar sesiones. Somos jefes de tribus escocesas que quieren expandir su reino. Durante seis rondas vamos a ir colocando unas losetas de terreno en nuestro territorio.

El inicio de cada una de estas rondas es donde realmente es interesante el juego. Cobramos dinero en función de cómo sea nuestro reino y de si tenemos jugadores por delante nuestro en la puntuación. Despues robaremos tres losetas que colocaremos delante nuestro. En secreto (usando una pantalla) marcaremos una de ellas para eliminarla y en las otras dos pondremos dinero (precio). Una vez todos estamos listos levantamos la pantalla y en orden de turno podemos comprar una loseta de otros jugadores. Cuando todos hayan comprado o no una loseta, las que nos queden delante nos las quedaremos pagando el dinero que hemos puesto como precio. De esto modo cuando todos hayan actuados tendremos de 0 a tres losetas para ampliar nuestro reino

Estas losetas tienen representados tipos de terreno (montañas, prados y agua) teniendo que seguir la coherencia de terrenos con las que ya tenemos colocadas. Además del terreno hay diferentes símbolos. Principalmente barriles, que si están unidos por caminos con nuestro castillo nos darán dinero al inicio del turno y luego animales, barcos y otros dibujicos que son necesarios para puntuar.

Porque al final de cada una de las rondas se van a puntuar entre una y tres cosas. Esas puntuaciones se saben al principio de la partida y permiten irte preparando para ellas. Cada una de las cuatro categorías se van a puntuar tres veces a lo largo de la partida, eso sí en diferentes rondas.

RATING: 7.5
Bokkensprong 16/9/16 met Ward (bijna 12), Jens De Meester, Luc, Ann en ik. Tegellegspel dat op het eerste zicht op Carcassonne lijkt, gespeeld in vijf ronden. Per ronde veranderen de scorecriteria. Elke speler trekt blind drie tegels uit de zak, legt ze open voor zich, en duidt in het geheim aan welke tegel terug in de zak moet, en wat de prijs is voor elk van de overige twee. Dan worden de kostprijzen openbaar gemaakt. In volgorde kunnen de spelers ev. één tegel elders kopen (en aanleggen), voor de prijs die werd bepaald door de verkoper. Tegels de niet gekocht worden, moeten worden betaald en aangelegd door de eigenaar. Er is een mechanisme ingebouwd waarbij spelers die in de laatste ronden achterop liggen, sterk worden bevoordeeld. De eindscores liggen daarom dicht bij elkaar. Leuk spel, met wel voortdurend wat telwerk voor de scores.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
2016-04-24

Initial Rating: 6.5 (April 2016)

Isle of Skye: From Chieftain to King was utterly unknown to me. When I saw the game was from Lookout Games my interest increased, but then I found the game was about tile laying (a mechanic I dislike most of the time) so I almost didn’t play it. However, I’m glad I did because I enjoyed it.

The game rules are very easy to learn. You can explain them under 15 minutes. Playtime is around 60 minutes.

The game components are very good and the art is good as well. When the game is on the table, it looks rather similar to Carcassonne, fortunately, it plays nothing like it.

It is weird that you can leave roads unconnected, but I welcome the novelty of it. 

Isle of Skye has a huge replayability factor because you play with 4 different goals each time and they appear in different order each time (I love this).

The game decisions aren’t obvious, in fact knowing how to value each of your tiles (similar to Key Harvest, a game I like) is very fun and it is the best feature of the game.

It is reminiscent of Between Two Cities (a game I also played for the first time this month) but Isle of Skye is superior in every way. Here you have meaningful decisions and luck is much more controlled.

On the bad side, I’m not a fan of artificial catch-up mechanisms, I don’t like that the last players get money for being behind, but it is a minor issue at the moment.

Isle of Skye has a light-medium weight, I was expecting a heavier game from Lookout Games.

Bottom line, I kind of like it, but my desire to play it again isn’t that high. That could change with more plays, but for now I won’t buy it. It is a good family game.

Current Rating: 6.0

RATING: 8
I'm Scottish. I couldn't resist. 

Fortunately it's a pretty good mix of auctions and Carcassonne-like tile laying, the key thing being you set the price of the tiles you want to sell, and you have to (or get to, depending on whether you really wanted the tile or not) pay the price you've set if no-one else will.

The tile laying's pretty simple, but the challenge is in managing to balance the points-scoring symbols on the tiles and how easy it is to place them as the location of the tiles (or more specifically, how they mesh together) as that's also significant for scoring.

Good mix of lightweight rules (takes about 5 minutes to explain) and middleweight tactics. I can't abide overwrought Euros that feel like algebra home work disguised as a game, but this one hits the mark for me.

That there are sixteen scoring tiles (only four which of are used in each game, with the order in which they're being played being significant) should add variety and give it longer legs.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Isle of Skye is a simple economic tile-laying game. Each turn, players draw three tiles, discard one, and using their own money, set a price on the other two tiles that other players are invited to match. Each player has the option of matching the price of one tile of a another player or passing. Any tiles that do not get selected are purchased by the seller for the price they had set. These tiles are added to a personal tableau or territory that is built very much like in Carcassonne. Before each game, four tiles are drawn that determine how tiles score throughout the game and you try to devise a territory that maximizes the scoring criteria for that particular game. 

It's a very straight-forward game that works for a lighter game night or for a family game night. Why do I like it? I have not seen another family game where players have to determine the value of a tile not just for themselves, but for other players as well. The key to the game is in the price-setting - knowing how high or low to set a price based on what you anticipate the demand for the tile to be. Price-setting is often a feature of heavy-weight economic games, but I have not seen it put to such excellent use in a package that can get my kids excited about doing something that I do all the time in games they that are not yet ready to appreciate. Playing this game has brought my kids closer to seeing games as I see them and valuing in them what I value. I hope their interest holds.

RATING: 9
Isle of Skye is brilliant for the way that the rules quickly get out of the way to let you play the game. Whether it's a result of playing off the familiarity of Carcassonne when it comes to tile placement, you become accustomed to what it is you have to do very quickly and it makes determining what tiles you're looking for easy to identify. And then when tiles start showing up with end-game bonuses or income potential, you start weighing the benefits of your choices... In any instance, I haven't had any problem with misunderstandings in this game; the sequence of play makes sense, the scoring tiles are brilliantly illustrated and easy to figure out - the only hurdle seems to be in the initial buying round and understanding where the money goes when someone buys a tile vs. when it's left in front of you after a buying round. Even so, it's still remarkably easy to grasp after playing through - it's just the unsupported explanation that throws people; which may mean it's more the way I'm explaining it than any issues with clarity in the rules. The wide variety of scoring tiles and the random order in how they come out increases replay-ability, giving each play a slightly different, yet no less impressive, flavour. Money well spent.

RATING: 7
A successful blend of known mechanisms but nothing outstanding. The most remarkable feature is the simultaneous hidden price setting for the drawn tiles. The rest is standard tile-laying with variable VP conditions each game increasing replay value.

While appealing, the pricing mechanism falls short of its potential. All item prices develop similarly across players from round to round with only slight differences between tiles depending on their usefulness in regard to the respective VP conditions selected for the game. I would have wished for a more differentiating experience. A surprising element is the implemented catch-up mechanism awarding players behind in VPs additional money. The sensible tactic is the Power Grid method to stay close behind the competitors to pass them on the last turn, aided here by a good money to VP conversion rate. I’m not sure why this mechanism is necessary. Potentially, the authors saw the possibility of players getting screwed by bad tile draws or perhaps it’s just an additional element to increase the depth of this light to midweight Euro.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Loving this game after two plays. You purchase tiles and place them in your kingdom based on a variety of possible scoring opportunities. You have end-of-round scoring as well as end-of-game scoring so which tiles you get and how you place them can earn you points as you go. I also love the purchasing mechanic, where each person selects three tiles each round and places them for all players to see. Each player secretly sets the price for each tile (with their actual money) and also chooses one tile to discard, leaving you with two tiles for sale. Then everyone reveals the prices they set for each tile and each player has the opportunity to buy one tile from another player. The buyer pays the price to the owner, and then the owner not only gets the paid amount, but they also keep the money that they set for that tile price. It becomes a delicate balance between what you think other players may want to buy and not letting it go too cheaply, because any tile that isn't purchased by another player becomes one that you keep, and you lose the money that you set the price with. Very tasty.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
10
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.6
Based on the fact it's a tile-laying game with tiles somewhat similar to that of Carcassonne, this game is often compared to Carcassonne. In my eyes the two are fundamentally different.
At its core, Isle of Skye has the Biblios/Merchants of Amsterdam mechanism without the plush your luck but with more planning: you draw three tiles, discard one, offer two for fixed-price 'auctions' (of which 0, 1 or 2 might be sold), buy the remaining one(s) and place them (along with the ones you bought from others) in your tableau. This is what the game is aboout, and the scoring options, of which there are 16 (besides a few set collection ones that are shown on certain cards), 5 of which don't even have any spatial aspect. But luckily the rest do, just like the placement rules (which do differ from that of Carcassonne) and the rules for gaining gold in the beginning of your game, so even if the central mechanism of the game is not about tile laying, in the end it is very important. And that's good, as I like tile-laying, even though I prefer games where you lay these tiles in one common 'map' and not just in your own tableau.
So, how is the game? It's fine. Yes, it might have a bit more scoring options (within any one game) than necessary but it's just as many as in Kingdom Builder which the variability of the game reminds of.
Also, after one or two plays it does play fast - our third, 2-player game (we played it with Saci again on Saturday) lasted only 30 minutes. It's good as in a 2-player play I think the role of luck can be a bit high (too many cards remain in the bag without even drawing them once, also there are two few cards at the opponent you can choose from). However, the game is quite well scaled for different player numbers: even though probably 4-player is ideal and 3 is very good, it seems to be good for 5 as well (having one fewer rounds was introduced so that each player can be the starting player once, but it also shortens the game that could otherwise become a bit long for what it is). So we played 3- and 2-player and I enjoyed both; I'm ready to explore it further.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
FIRST IMPRESSION:

I have a hard time not thinking of roads as borders for determining areas but only land types count (land, sea, mountains). The price setting aspect reminds me a bit of Key Harvest but this one can be nasty in that you could theoretically end up with no tiles to place if you choose not to buy one and people buy the two tiles you put out for sale. Some tiles also involve end game scoring aspects. Plays quickly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Jeder Spieler erhält 3 Carcassonne-Artige Plättchen und legt sie vor sich. Hinter seinem Sichtschirm definieren alle jewels den Preis zweier Plättchen und eines das abgeworfen wird. Es fogt eine Kaufrunde. Beginnend beim Startspieler darf sich jeder kein bis ein Plättchen kaufen. Alle Plättchen die dann noch vor einem liegen muss man selbst kaufen. Danach dürfen alle gekauften Plättchen in sein Königreich (Carcassonne Regeln im Grunde) eingebaut werden und man erhält Siegpunkte, die von der aktuellen Runde und der Siegpunktplättchenauslage abhängig ist. Es folgt die zweite Runde. Nach 5 Runden ist es auch schon vorbei und es gibt einen Geinner bzw. eine Gewinnerin.
Ich finde den Bietmechnismus sehr gut, da ich mich in Andere hineinversetzen muss, was sie zahlen würden oder eben nicht mehr.
Das Plättchen auslegen ist nichts spannendes, aber was mich stört ist die Siegpunktevergabe. Diese ist jede Runde ein wenig anders und nicht so klar wie bei Carcassonne. Der Wiederspielreiz erhöht sich durch die wechselnden Siegpunkteplättchen, aber mir gefällt das nicht. Jeder spielt auch auf seinem Hofplan und es gibt keine gemeinsame Auslage.
Im Grunde ein gutes funktionierendes Spiel, aber ich spiele wirklich lieber Carcassonne (oder ein anderes Spiel), wobei ich mich auch nicht sträube weiter mitzuspielen.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Unique bidding mechanic where you draw three tiles and place a price behind a screen and select one tile to "axe".  After you reveal you can buy one tile from another player based on the price set. You buy your own tiles with the price set if no one buys your tile.  

Tiles are placed Carcassonne style.  There are four random scoring conditions that score three times over six rounds.

The bidding is interesting with the closed economy.  My initial reaction is a 5.  But the game plays quickly.  I would be willing to try this game again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An interesting blend of mechanics that mostly works.  I like the variable victory conditions.  The auction price setting is obscure to me.  And the money catch up mechanism seems odd in a game with so much end and late game scoring opportunities.  The biggest issue I have is with some graphic design choices which especially clash with the superficial similarity to Carcassonne.

RATING: 7.5
This is a great family tile laying / head scratching working out the economic value of tiles game. It works well with all level of gamers and all player counts. Every game will be different due to the 16 different scoring tiles and that you only use 4 of them in each game. This will change up each game drastically and hugely adds to replayability.

The artwork is just as good as the smooth gameplay and as a result it is extremely easy to teach and both of my younger kids have picked up the game with no problems. But being good at it is another matter!

The game is played over 6 rounds and the main board will tell you which of the scoring tiles are scored at the end of each round. Each player takes 3 tiles from a bag (think Carcassonne tiles and you can't go wrong) and places them in front of their Scottish clan screen (did I forget to mention you play as Scottish clans and there are whiskey barrels, lighthouses, ships and ... what look like yaks, all the usual Scottish stuff, could almost be a game of Glen More another personal favourite). Each player then chooses secretly safely behind their castle walls which of the three tiles to discard and puts a monetary value (using the actually money they have at their disposal) on the other two tiles. Then all is revealed and in player turn you have the option to buy one of the other players tiles at the price they have set (but no you can't use the money you used to set the value of your tiles, that would be way to easy!). If no one chooses to buy your tile(s) you have to at the cost you priced it at paying with the money you used to set its value.

This method of pricing and acquiring tiles is fantastic and meaty for such a smooth running game. Money flows to and from you throughout the game and is in a constant changing state of flux. A bit like real life except in that it mainly seems to flow away from me and bits of colourful cardboard in boxes flows back!

The tile laying itself is just as good. Each game will force you to create very different lands depending on the scoring tile requirements.

This is just a great game and no description can do its simplicity, meatiness, variability and delightful turns of economy justice.

If you game with your family try this. If you play with mainly experienced gamers try this. It is like a lemon sorbet fizzing on your palette after a huge meal of chateaubriand. Light and delicious, it reaffirms your belief that you enjoy gaming and could wolf down some more.

Great game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
What a great game! Pricing the tiles just right is challenging and rewarding. What if the tile is too good for the other player? What if they don't have enough money? Only con is that the game might be too long with AP prone people. And it really punishes people who aren't good at the game, because you might need to plan for all kinds of tiles, when building your landscape. Still, this just gets better every time you play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is everything I wanted it to be: Carcassonne-like, but with a tight round limit, variable scoring, and an auction mechanic. I absolutely love how rich with decisions it is.

Every single choice has a branching tree of possible consequences, and while that meant we took it past its 60-minute listed play time by thinking too long during bidding, it also meant that every decision was interesting. (A second play with the same group would have gone a lot faster, I suspect. It needs a learning game.) As a huge fan of turn angst, I love this game.

I'm also a fan of games I enjoy losing, and I went from doing well, to first, to dead last in my first game of IoS -- and had a blast the whole time. At the midgame, we were all within 2 points of each other; towards the end, the two players with the strongest, best-executed strategy pulled way ahead. I drove home thinking about things I could have done differently, and how to approach it next time; always a good sign.

I'm also looking forward to dealing four random scoring conditions next time (rather than the preset "first game four"), and to how much variety and replayability that introduces into an already varied and interesting game.

RATING: 6.5
Game in a Nutshell: Players set up a market of tiles for purchase then play them into a puzzle to score in various ways.

Played as: 5 player

Pros: Very intuitive game rules-wise while still offering some variability in which scoring tiles are used and offering some gut wrenching choices when pricing the tiles in your market.

Cons: Those gut-wrenching choices when pricing your tiles can slow things down. Also, each round ends in scoring a mini point-salad (more so in later rounds) which seems to take a fair chunk of the game's playing time. I'm probably overstating those two time sinks in the grand scheme of this not being that long of a game, but I'd say 2/3 of the play time is on those two parts of the game. I was really excited going into the game about the tile laying aspect, but found the interesting aspects of that mechanic neutered leaving nothing more then a somewhat obvious jigsaw puzzle. And for some reason, I never got that end of game sense of "look what I built!"

Other: Reading the above, would make someone think I didn't like the game all that much, but it really is nice. It's probably a result of delivering a product, not necessarily worse, but different then my expectations. It's really a very solid piecing together of established designs. I expect I might like the game more with fewer players (only played with the max 5 players) as scoring and surveying a smaller market would reduce those more drawn out element of the game. 

Best Use: A shorter "buy-tiles-in-a-common-market-then build-your-own-puzzle-with-them" game (think Vegas Showdown, Princes of Florence, Castles of Mad King Ludwig, etc.).

☛ Lo tengo desde que salió en inglés y me encanta. En septiembre de 2015 predije que podía ser nominado a los SdJ (finalmente -y todavía no entiendo por qué- lo ha sido en la categoría "Kenner" -para expertos- y creo que no ganará estando emparejado con bestias pardas como Pandemic Legacy y T.I.M.E Stories). Celebro su edición en castellano.

✔ La combinación de objetivos diferentes a puntuar cada partida lo hacen muy rejugable.
✔ La duración de una partida está muy bien ajustada.
✔ El reglamento es impecable a la par que sencillo.
✔ Se puede sacar tanto a aficionados a los juegos como a neófitos.
✔ La parte de tasar losetas para comprarlas y/o conservarlas genera una interacción orgánica muy agradable.
✔ La calidad de los componentes y de la producción son el paradigma de juego accesible, usable y rejugable típico de los Spiel des Jahres.

✘ La rejugabilidad que ofrecen los objetivos de puntuación combinables tiene un doble filo: en determinadas ocasiones las partidas pueden resultar algo monótonas si todos los objetivos son similares.
✘ Se aplica una mecánica de compensación (en forma de monedas) para los jugadores que van más rezagados en el track de puntuación al final de cada ronda. Eso puede no gustar a determinados jugadores. 
✘ A cuatro jugadores, los dos primeros serán "jugador inicial" dos veces durante la partida y los dos últimos sólo una. Discutí en la BGG con los autores sobre si esto podía afectar al juego y convenimos que no... pero aún así creo que se podría haber resuelto de una manera más elegante.

RATING: 8
I knew I was going to like this game before I even played it. Each player are building up a landscape akin to what you do in Carcassonne, except instead of drawing a single tile out of a bag and placing it, you draw three, and you in secret give one of them the axe and the other two a price. You have to balance the prices you set with how much money you decide to hold back, as once players remove their player screens, they each get a chance to purchase one of the other players' tiles. If you decide to purchase a tile, you pay the other player the price they have set for the tile, and they get to pick back up the money they themselves had put down on that tile - giving them that much more purchase power either in this game round or the next.

Once all players have gotten the chance to purchase a tile or pass, the money they have put down on the tiles their opponents didn't buy from them goes to the bank and they add the tiles they have purchased to their landscape.

Scoring varies from round to round as the game has four different scoring condition (drawn from a decent stack of scoring tiles) that triggers in a different combination with each round, making players weigh the importance of gaining points in the current round against building up to score big in the next round. You also have to weigh whether it's better to go for a round-scoring tile (all scoring conditions are triggered three times in a game) or a scroll that will give you points at the end of the game. There's also an interesting handicap bonus in the game, where players receive an increasing amount of money for every player ahead of them in the game with every passing round.

I've played this game both as a 2-player game and as a 5-player game and I've found both versions enjoyable, but very different in terms of strategy. A 2-player game is naturally very back and forth, and you are guaranteed to always at least have one tile to add to your landscape. While in a 5-player game, there are no guarantees you will keep any of your given tiles and you have to rely more on keeping enough money on hand to make a purchase. 

I really enjoy the deeper, yet simple enough to grasp strategy in this game, as it gives a fresh take on the tile-laying mechanic.

RATING: 9
Nice strategy game, played only with two players. Games last ~40 minutes.

+ Graphics on cards. Nice to the eyes, relaxing cards.
+ Quality of all items.
+ A great variance in strategies in each game due to the random A-B-C-D cards in each game.
+ Even more variety in strategy due to the extra VP papyrus and the ability to "close" lands.
+ Every game feels different due to the above points.

- In a two player game, the +3 coins to the one behind, can be a bit overpowering, if the difference is only 1 VP.
- In a >2 player game, you can be left with no cards in your hands in a turn if not planned correctly.
- Luck can still be a deciding factor.
