RATING: 8
Considering the low price for this game I'd recommend buying it immediately without really thinking why. It's a great game to enjoy, especially with the family and is both intriguing and when you get to the end of the game in most cases you'll be surprised at how fast it went. Time only flies when you're having fun and since that's what happened to my gaming group this game is a lot of fun! And why get board games if not for fun?
Every player gets some tiles at the beginning of their turn which they either try to sell by giving them relatively low prices (or medium at most) or they plan to buy themselves and so give them a higher cost. If someone buys your tiles they pay you the amount of money you would have paid for the tile and you get to keep your money. If nobody buys your tiles you'll have to pay the amount of money you would have paid and the tile is yours. Players then try to build a small kingdom by both getting more whiskey barrels in order to receive more money each turn and they try to get the specific animals, structures, etc in their little kingdom, depending on the current missions.
There are a lot of fun elements in this game and even though I've never played Carcassonne I bet that Isle of Skye is better than it since it both has the interesting element of building your kingdom (but it also has a lot of different kinds of animals, buildings, etc on the tiles which all do something as well) and the fascinating concept of buying and selling. Not good at board games? Then this is the game for you (even though it's still a great game if you are good at board games)! Players who are behind receive more money which is very important in the game and then get a chance to catch up. It's a very family friendly game, quite simple but can be a little confusing since you have to concentrate on at least four different objectives. All in all, no need for words. It's both interesting AND cheap!

RATING: 6
I like the auction mechanic a lot, it is very original in an otherwise pretty standard tile laying game. I like the changing score tiles, but some combinations are less interesting than others. In the end I like Carcasonne better with more interaction and without the auction and catch up mechanism.

RATING: 7.95

I really like the auction part of the game... it's great to have to look at what other players have that you might want to grab... look at the tiles in front of you and decide how to price them in terms of how much you want them or how much you know others will want them and also to decide which tile to ace from the game...

RATING: 10
This is what Carcassonne should have been (then again, Carcassonne was released a lot of years earlier). Also, this makes me wish the rating could go higher... I'm not going to reclick everything else a few degrees lower, but boy, Skye is a gem deserving an 11 at least.
Why?
First of all, having to connect paths in Carcassonne buggered me immensely (it's not an area of one color, merely a line, plus we don't consider a real meadow to be two different meadows just because there's a pathway in the middle) and this game neatly combines a little bit of set collection into the mix, while mitigating luck of the tile draw, because the players can purchase tiles from one another. Might be even a tad short for 2 players, but boy the decisions are agonizing on such simple rules!!!
Also, only using 4 scoring tiles of 16 possible (where the order also matters) makes sure the game is going to be replayable a ton. I had to try several games just to see HOW MUCH is the gameplay and decisions different just because different things are scored this time around. Great job in game design!

A little bit AP-prone game, but as usual, that mainly depends on the gaming group. Killer of: Carcassonne, Mad King Ludwig.
With my constant alternating of two places of residence, this is one of the few games where I didn't regret the money to own it twice.

Recommended: in 2 or 3 players each round draw one "dummy player tile", only one, to be purchasable from the bank for [round number] + 1 coins. Do not redraw it within one round (only one such tile per round, 6 per game), always redraw between rounds (even when not bought).

RATING: 7
I've found this one always gets a positive response from players--it's much fiddlier than Carcassonne but nonetheless it's a nice way to introduce meatier gaming to casual gamers familiar with Carcassonne. (Just as this game would be a good gateway to Keyflower.) -- My major complaint is that while CONCEPTUALLY the game is elegant, PHYSICALLY it's a bit awkward. You need a lot of table space for all the isles to expand, but the result is that everyone sits really far away from each other, and trying to peer at all the tiles up for auction and discern the features on them and the money is very hard. I think in future I will try to rearrange the table so that tiles for auction go in the centre, with some kind of marker to show who put them up for auction. -- That said, the way that money flows easily in this game, and the limit of buying exactly one tile per turn, means that the game is a lot more user-friendly than an auction game like Keyflower--this is a marketplace game rather than an auction game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
As chieftan of a Scottish clan, each turn you draw three tiles out of a bag. You have to decide which 2 tiles to auction, and which one to throw away. But if the tiles you auction at the price you set are not bought, you must buy them yourself and put them into your tableau.

Depending on the number of players, there are a number of scoring rounds. Each game, there are 4 things that score (out of 16 possible scoring markers, so each game will be quite different).  Each round will be a combination of the 4 possible, so you want to accumulate tiles towards that end. 

It's a fun game. Comparisons have been made to Carcassone, but I feel it is more strategic, and more interactive. There is a lot going on here, and tryign to price your tiles right for the auction is tough.  You do need to think what tiles other players want, and the price they will pay, and you have to decide if you want to try to keep those tiles yourself.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The cardboard is thick and the art is by Klemens Franz: practical but not georgeous. An important detail: the scoring tiles are variable and you only use 4 in each game. A standard game would have included 8 of these tiles to ensure variability, 10 at most. Isle of Skye includes 20 scoring tiles.

It's pretty much the base rules of Carcassone with a couple of twists. But what twists! The auction is the core of the game and gives sense to everything else. The powerful valiant catch-up mechanisms, the possibility of destroying one tile, the changing scoring condictions... The game doesn't lose the simplicity of Carcassonne, but it gains so much... I wouldn't say it's much deeper, but you certainly take more important decisions that will define your strategy and the map-building (those things never happened in Carcassonne).

The luck factor has been dampened: you get 3 tiles and choose 2. You can buy your rival's tiles and they can buy yours. There's still luck there (if you get a great tile, you'll either keep it or receive much money), but now it's just a factor more, it's not more important than the rest.

The interaction between players is constant, both in the auction and with the catch-up mechanisms. The auction is a bit tricky and AP prone players should approach it carefully.

My other issue with this game is the catch-up mechanism. I understand its purpose, I know it's imperative because otherwise there will be a perennial problem of runaway leader. But I think it might be too powerful. I need more gaming time to be sure.

Fist impression [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/184429/item/4279704#item4279704]here[/url].

RATING: 8
[b]Isle of Skye: From Chieftain to King[/b] was given to Mat.

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8 -> [b]8[/b]):[/COLOR]

[i]While Isle of Skye definitely stood the test of time for me, my family saw otherwise. Unfortunately they did not like the combination of tile laying and having to bid for tiles/manage your income. We didn't play enough for them to get a feel for how to value the tiles and having turns where they didn't have a tile to place left them enjoying the game even less. I ended up giving my copy to a buddy of mine, but haven't had a chance to play since. Isle of Skye is definitely a game I'd enjoy playing more, but it'll have to be with my game group.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]Isle of Skye provides a great mash up of tile laying and bidding/$ management. My family compared it to Carcassonne with the addition of having to buy your tiles. Yes the tiles you buy are extremely important to winning, but after a couple of plays you realize how important it is to manage your $ and properly price your tiles. 

There is a lot of player interaction since a tile you really wanted gets bought by another player, you could potentially end up not placing a single tile on your turn and that doesn't feel good. The odds of that happening decrease once you become familiar with how the game works so I don't consider it a negative.

Isle is an excellent choice if you're looking for a game with a great play time to depth ratio. There is a lot of tough decisions to be made in a 45 minute game. The large amount of scoring tiles (of which only 4 are chosen each game) assures plenty of replay. I hope to play this a lot more but the jury is still out for the rest of my family; although they did our second play much more than the first.[/i]

2.每人抽三個版塊放在屏幕前，並在屏幕後面對應位置選擇兩個放錢，一個放棄置版塊
3.對應棄置版塊的丟回袋中
4.起始玩家開始輪流最多可以跟別人買一個板塊(價格為該玩家的定價)，收錢的同時把訂價的錢拿回手，另外一塊板塊就要自己付錢買

6.回合結束根據圖版對應計分板塊計分，每塊會計三次分，換起始玩家

RATING: 7
It has me thinking of Carcassonne:The Discovery being played using the core Key Harvest mechanic of placing tiles out with a cost attached, where someone will either buy it (plus you get your money back), or you get to keep it but you lose your money. You can only score by getting tiles out, so setting the price right to be discouraging and finding bargains elsewhere seems to be the key, but balancing that with maintaining cashflow to keep and buy the tiles you need. I like how the means of scoring points changes from game to game, and varies from round to round which causes valuation fluctuation. Not so much a fan of the time investment required to study each player's board to determine the value of your tiles to each player, and whether your tile is of primary interest at what price to each player - the Mad Ludwig issue. And I suspect most players can't be bothered and do it by feel, which probably leads to some accidental king-making. Leading me to the conclusion that fewer players is probably best so you can find a nice halfway house. But otherwise it's pleasing simplicity provided interesting decisions, it was engaging throughout, and had a nice comfort level due to its similarity in art and game feel to Carcassonne.

RATING: 6.5
This is a tile placement game, but unlike a lot of these games you each have your own area to place tiles. You get to pull 3 tiles from the bag and then put two for sale and one you put back in the bag. Each player has the option then to buy one tile at the noted prices however any tiles not purchased you must pay the amount you set them at. Using the 4 previously selected scoring tiles, each round you score from 1 to 3 of them so you can plan for the current ones but also since each scores 3 times you can aim to score a lot in all or focus on a few right from the start. A simple game that would be a good introduction but for me it is too simple and I prefer heavier games of this type.

RATING: 7
Isle of Skye is a tile-laying game in which players draw a hand of tiles and must price them for both their opponents and themselves.  The opponents get the first crack at tiles in the single buying round, and any unbid tile must be bought by the player who offered it.  There are a number of public goals that are revealed at the beginning of the game, but they activate at the end of different rounds, making their value vary as the game progresses.  There are also a few private goals printed on some tiles.  The pricing and buying mechanic can lead to some analysis paralysis, especially at larger player counts.  On the whole, though, this was enjoyable.

RATING: 8.3
(01/!8)

Designer Alexander Pfister's take on Tile Laying and "Bidding" is a great little puzzle that almost never repeats itself. I think I've never had a game of IoS that felt solved from the beggining, actually, one of my playing friends says he hates it, because he can't figure it out and feels he doesn't know what he's doing.

I feel like this game is about letting go of a plan, and just go with what you have and adapt. 

The bidding mechanic is so tense... "am I putting too much money on this tile? or maybe too little so they'll buy it, maybe it is too little... nooo, too much"

and the nothing turns out the way you want it, but you have to keep going.

I almost always want to play it. It is easy to teach to people that have played Carcassone.

This game isn't a thinker, but it is not light, there's so much silence on the table with this game, but it never feels tiring.

RATING: 7
Got to play this game at this month's local board game meetup. 

It is essentially a tile laying game but with an buying mechanism that reminds me of Castles of Mad King Ludwig. You get randomized scoring conditions and you try to work the best to your ability to score the most points. 

After 2 rounds of buying how to set up price becomes very cloudy since you have to consider both your own and all your opponent's board to set the price and sometimes I wonder if money (1 pt per $5 at end of game) is really necessary in this game. Money doesn't feel tight in this game compared to CoMKL or other Euros but maybe it is because I've played a 3-player game?

I need to work on my spatial brain power though. I sucked at my first play through.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Easy, quick, wonderful.

Draw random tiles, put them up for sale. Set the price.
If others buy them from you, you get the money but lose the tile. If they don't, you get the tile but lose the money. Delicious decisions.

Scoring is different every game. You can set yourself up for a good score by creating opportunities for tiles to be worth much more to you than to your opponents, so you can get them (for cheap).

What I like most is that it has a solo puzzle element - when you're agonizing over the decision how much money to place on your tiles and how much to keep for purchasing - but the game is still almost 100% player interaction: everything the others do is important to you. And the solo bit you do simultaneously, so the game doesn't drag.

Also impressive is that the game gives a huge bonus to players that are behind, and it doesn't unbalance the game. Even if they have 40 cash to spend and you have 5, you're still playing the same game. Trying to keep your lead with limited options is a fun challenge, as is making use of all your bonus wealth to catch up to the leaders.

Different combinations of scoring elements each game - from placing your tiles in squares to creating the largest lake possible to simply getting as many sheep as you can - means the game is different each time.

Skye reminds me somewhat of Ra. Valuate the tiles, from your own position and those of your opponents, and increase your score while strengthening your relative position at the table.

It's been some time since I found a new '10' game. Skye is exactly what I was looking for.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
P: 2-5, T: 45 min, KenSdJ-W
+Tile laying (a la Carc) with clever bidding on tile purchases. Random round scoring (goals), tile specific scoring, and player order penalty/bonus keeps strategic and tactical planning tense and tremendously variable. 
-Deep decision making, can be AP prone. The game doesn't quite have the tile laying charm I expected. The key decisions seem to revolve around how to price the tiles and what to buy. Not as much a gateway as others seem to regard it. The balance of the came can suffer greatly if one of the players fails to grasp how point scoring and maximization is realized for a particular setup.
I think other reviews have exaggerated the impact of the catch up mechanism (and their criticism for its presence in this game). While I generally approve of catch-up mechanics, a few extra coins equates to a minor victory point bump, and most players who can't appropriately price tiles aren't served well by a few more coins.

While many consider this to be a medium-light family game accessible and satisfying to both casual and strategic gamers, I consider the weight to be considerably higher. The depth of decisions regarding long/short term goals (round scoring, tile placement, tile pricing, tile axing, evaluating vp value of each tile respective of when it becomes available, evaluating vp value of tiles relative to different players), analyzing other player's needs/goals, adaptive strategies (bonus scoring scroll tiles), and money management make this game packed with decisions. I think experience and analytical players will be able to approach mastery of game mechanics and create large scoring disparities (as our sessions have indicated).
I worry our group will find it a bit too dense have long-term repeated play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a tile laying game, where each round, each player gets three tiles. They select one of the tiles to be removed from the game, and set the buy price of the other two. Then in turn order, each player has the opportunity to buy one tile from any other player, paying them the cost of the tile. At the end of the buy phase, any tiles not bought, you pay for yourself to the bank, and add all the tiles to your little personal tableau, following the placement rules of land types on the edges of the tiles need to match up. Then there's a scoring. The scoring criteria are different every game, which gives the game a ton of replayability. This game really is fantastic. It's short, meaty and has a ton of player interaction. 

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

The variable scoring is inspired, and will certainly extend the play life of the game.  The components are pretty typically Mayfair: higher quality, solid art, but built for use more than looking gorgeous on the table.  Overall, a pretty great game value, and a nice spin on a classic.

RATING: N/A
Pros:
* The auction mechanism works perfectly with 2p
* Quick setup / cleanup
* Fun!
* Mathy puzzles to solve while to price and place tiles
* Great art work and good quality components.

Cons:
* Apparently it's pretty common, in a 2p game, for luck-of-the-tile-draw to screw one player.
* Lots of little calculations and if you want to really get an idea how much you should value a tile, you need to carefully look around at all of the other boards.
* Could be AP prone

Neutral:
* I generally dislike games with player screens, but as a method to hide you choices rather than your resources, I think it worked well. Reminds me of Fresco where you decide what you're going to do and then do a big reveal.

= 2015-10-22

Some comments after a single 2p game. My first play involved two scoring tiles that gave points for having groupings including light houses. I acquired two lighthouses early in the game and then no other opportunities ever came up for my opponent to acquire one (a few more lighthouse tiles came up, but always on my side and I just axed them). After a very lopsided game, we looked over the list of tiles and figured those may be two of the worst tiles in the game for 2p and we ended up with both of them.

But the important thing to take away from this is that we both still very much enjoyed the game. As frustrating as it was for F to be have absolutely no way to get a lighthouse and as anticlimactic the final turn (the last tiles were so boring, I put one up for sale for a single coin and it didn't get purchased), we still had a lot of fun. There's a game here and it's much more than just getting the most points. It's fun.

I was worried that it would be hard to value the tiles and it would require a few plays to really get an idea what they're worth, but even if we mis-priced a few things here and there, it didn't feel like anyone got a steal of a deal. Either you get a tile or you get some money; either way, this isn't one of those annoying blind auction games that leaves you feeling cheated by lady luck (at least not for the auction part).

No rating yet as I don't think my first play was very typical.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Pros:
1. Decision Points. This game requires the tough decision of how to price your tiles, how much do you keep back to buy other tiles, and which tile do you axe. It's such a simple mechanic that requires some hard decision making.
2. Variability. There are many different scoring tiles that will change every game. This changes the feel of the game every time you play.
3. Quick to Play. When everyone knows the rule, a 4 player game is less than 40 minutes.
4. Easy to Teach. The game takes approximately 10 minutes to teach.

Cons:
1. Catch Up Mechanic. The catch up mechanic is simply giving out gold to those who are further behind. When the scores are close, the same amount of gold is given out even if they're further apart. This catch up mechanic has caused massive swings in the final round.


Summary: This is an amazing game. The variability and tough decision points keep this game fresh. This game works for heavy euro players, new gamers and families. The versatility makes this game a must have in my collection.

RATING: 9
Wow, just wow - that's how you make a great gateway eurogame! For me, this game shows how bland and boring are designs like the hugely overrated (at least from my perspective) "Splendor".

"Isle of Skye" has everything a gateway needs. Rules? Three minutes of explanation. Setup time? Almost non-existant. Ease of play? Certainly. Add to that no downtime and what you get is one of the best Spiel des Jares contender in recent years. This game offers tons of replayability and considerable depth, if you factor in how easy and simple it is. It plays fast, has interesting interaction in the very clever "auction" mechanic. No turn feels unimportant, you'll be engaged from the beginning till the very end. Bought it, unpacked, and played 8 times in a row. And that says something!

Definitely a keeper, will be my go-to gateway game next to titles like TtR and "Kingdom Builder".

RATING: N/A
I played this once, with four other players.

It's quite quick and a clever, streamlined design. Fun mechanisms such as 'push-your-luck' etc are baked-into the simple actions. It will reward smart play.

However, this game also takes things to an extreme regarding making you squint across the table to constantly add-up what combinations of icons your opponents have on their (evolving) tableaus of cards. The entire game is built around that, as your actions are all about selling to them what they need. Unfortunately this means about 20% of the game is actual tactics/strategy and 80% is a web of simple math, which puts me off. May the top squinter/adder win!

If I did score it, it might be a 6/10. That's 8/10 for slick design but two points deducted for that problematic game format.

RATING: 8.5
This is a strange combo of Carcassonne with a bidding feel of Revolution.  But what makes this unique is that the blind bidding has multiple layers.  You not only bid to maybe keep a tile but you also set a price for other to buy.  On top of that you also get to get rid of a tile completely.  If this is enough to wrap your head around but you then must use these tile to score victory points based on different criteria each round. The rules are super streamlined but the game is very deep and satisfying.  The victory tiles change each game and there are a tons of them so no two games really play out the same.   One of my favorite games of 2015.

RATING: 5
Played this with 2 players and quite honestly I could not play this with more than two. It just burns my brain, I kept needing to pick up my opponent's tiles and placing them in my territory to see if they would work or not before deciding if I wanted to buy them. Then I would do that with my own tiles. 

And some of the scoring felt fiddly to me, for example counting all the animals that are adjacent to farms (and diagonal) and then counting the animals again that were actually on a farm. Some of the scoring felt a bit tedious.

You need to slow down and pay attention during the buy phase. It's easy to accidentally take money that you should have paid the bank and vice versa because you are buying tiles and keeping tiles that no one else bought but on those you pay the money you put on them to the bank. It's not hard but it's easy to get confused if you're talking or whatever. Again, just has a fiddly feel to it.

This game is okay and it's not broken or anything, but I found it to be a bit dull. Felt like a KS. As far as tile laying games go I am much happier with Carc.

RATING: 7
Very good, price-setting game.  I much prefer the way this mechanic is used here than in Mad King Ludwig, since it's simultaneous and less AP-prone.  Secret price setting and then turn-based buying is much faster and actually more interesting than open auctions - it should be used more often.  The way each game will have a different combination of scoring goals adds some additional replay value, although I think most of the fun is in how much players choose to be generous or stingy in their pricing and how that evolves as they get more experienced.

Update: Still really enjoy the price-setting.  We had the 5 or 2 points for 1st/2nd most money goal, which adds a lot to your price-setting and purchasing decisions.  The scoring system of having different goals a la Suburbia is great - the timing system of when they score is just meh.  Apart from not caring about the A goal on the last round tile choices, it's not relevant.  However, I find this is another example of taking a great mechanic and then half-assing a game to use that mechanic.  I just want there to be something more thematic, more interesting, more interactive, in what's going on with the tiles.  

My idea is if we were architects designing buildings - if no one purchases and builds your designs, you have to build them yourself, and all the purchased tiles are added to a single growing city in the middle of the table, with personal tokens placed on them to indicate ownership.  What the buildings are and where/when they are placed provides income, points, and special abilities.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Good light bidding game that's easy to explain and quick to play. The auction system from Key Harvest has always appealed to me. This was on my wish list on that basis alone. Add that it's by the designer of Port Royal and I my interest was definitely piqued.

The flow of money with this auction system is interesting. Sometimes it's better to forego an expensive purchase to leave your opponents short of cash. So pricing is an interesting calculation and risk based upon tiles not yet pulled from the bag.

The game is hindered slightly by the simple turn order determination - start player moves to the player on the left. Turn order is very important for your cashflow. As players cotton on to this they start to factor this in and starve the last buying player of funds by not buying from them. Well, it's not super important, but especially early in the game, as funds are scarce, it seems like a bit of a random element decided by the luck of your seating position.

Of course it doesn't work so well with only two players, but from 3-5 it's interesting without being brain burning. Along with Cacao from 2015 it's been a welcome addition to my light games shelf.

RATING: 5
I felt like the tile buying mechanism creates an illusion of players interactions in a mostly solitaire game. Most of the time, I'm just looking at my own tiles and assign $ to each tile based on how important they are to my current board. There's no way I would have the time to look at each of my opponent's board and account for which tiles I should buy from them to screw them over. I also do not have the time to look at everyone's board and see which of my tiles are important to an opponent (and react by placing more $ on my own tiles). Ultimately, what my opponents do, while could potentially affect my game, is not something I can influence.

In the end I think luck plays a major factor in this game and winning the game didn't feel fulfilling. As a filler game, I think it's longer than it needs to be.

RATING: 6

There are elements of Carcassonne in that placement rules are visual, but tiles are auctioned off in fixed price auctions and players build their tableaus individually. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
1 play, 4er

Interesting tile-laying set-collection game with variable point scoring. You start the game poor but can fairly quickly build up a pile of cash -- and set prices accordingly higher and higher. You have to acquire tiles to score them, of course, so you have to price yours high enough that they both aren't consistently bought out from under you every turn! I have a hard time with that part, although I still had fun with the game.

To set up, 4 scoring tiles are randomly selected each game from a large pool. Each of these will score thrice over the course of the 6 rounds each game. Tiles have a variety of features (landscape types, roads, building/animal/other icons). Players start with a home tile. Each round, players first collect income (5 + 1 per connected whisky icon). Then, they simultaneously (and secretly) draw 3 random tiles: discard one, set a price (using your own coins) on the other two -- all relieved simultaneously. Then, in player order, either buy a tile from someone ELSE (they get your coins, and the coins they used to set the price) or pass. Then, add your purchased tiles (including any you put up that went unsold -- you buy them at cost) to your tableau. End the round by scoring the 1-3 types of scoring indicated for the current round. 

After 6 rounds, everyone also scores:
- 1VP per 5coins
- any VP indicated on the tiles in their tableau (by scrolls; these are doubled if the scroll is in a terrain that is completely enclosed). 
Most VP wins.

RATING: 8
Take note Carcassone fans!  This is a well designed tile-laying, map-building game.  I hate Carcassone.  I think it is a terrible, poorly designed game and can't for the life of me understand the universal love for it and consideration as a great, classic game.  Anyway, this is a game about drawing tiles from a bag and building a map, completing landscape types for points.  But this one I enjoyed quite a bit.

So each turn players draw three tiles from a bag, one of them is "axed" and sent back to the bag, the other two you place money on.  This sets both the price for other players as well as the price you will pay for the tile yourself if no other player purchases it.  This is very weird and makes no sense thematically/logically (in fact I was convinced we made a mistake with the rule because it makes so little sense) but mechanically this is a very interesting way to handle auctions.  Once the tile auction phase is finished you put the tiles into your personal map.  Terrain types need to match (except roads) and you score points based on universal goals as well as bonuses on many of the tiles.

I think my one complaint which happens a lot with many games with universal goals is that they aren't all created equal.  We had one goal in our game that earned players 10-12 points a turn while the others were only granting 5-6.  This makes it so there are certain goals you need to aim for and so things like luck of the draw and turn order can give players somewhat unfair advantages.  Because of the auction phase and number of options this isn't too bad though.

Other than that I really enjoyed the game and the level of variety and replayability it appears to offer.  It looks nice and colorful and it also plays really quickly (without too terribly AP players, that is).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
There is no denying that Carcassonne has been one of the most influential games of modern board gaming. Isle of Skye, on the other hand, is said to follow up on the same ideas, only adding a few elements (such as an auction phase) to make for  deeper element.

As such, Isle of Skye is still an easy to teach and learn game, but it is deep enough to make for an interesting experience. Also, it can be played in less than one hour. It's decent. Nothing more, nothing less.

RATING: 4
Carcassonne crossed with something like the offering from Key Harvest: draw tiles and set prices; take the payment from the bank if someone else buys or pay your price to the bank and place it yourself.

Worth a look if you're after Carc++, though the individual tableaus mean significantly less interaction.

RATING: 5.5
Friend of mine brought his copy to the gaming table. It is kind of a fun diversion, but I found out, like I did when Carcassonne so many years ago, that tile-laying games simply do not hold my attention. The price setting is nice, but too fiddly because of the too small player boards provided. The availability of what's up is a random draw, the availability of what does not get axed is out of your control. You are left with only setting your own price and after that the game basically runs itself. You either maximise your own score or try to hamper a runaway leader. And in a 4p game, the smart choice is always the maximisation.

I tried to go for long-term strategy, maximizing the end-game bonuses and grabbing a surprise win, and found out the hard way that

a) you don't have to include these bonuses in the area you like to score
b) it doesn't matter anyway as they are just tie-breakers to a victory: the experienced player (the friend bringing the copy) immediately grasped the superior scoring mechanism drawn up and gobbled up the good tiles for it. After that, the game was dead.

Don't see this hitting our table very often again, and don't mind it not doing so.

RATING: 7.2
tl;dr: Carcassonne with a bidding mechanism and no meeples

It's a tile laying game, so of course you will see comparisons with the most popular title in the genre, but it is quite similar in many ways. You also have multiple elements and landscape types for which you can score and the tiles look quite similar. But you don't need meeples to score anything. And you don't just draw and place, but you auction tiles (for a set price). And most of all: you all build your own tableau. So it is not Carcassonne, but it does feel like it in many respects.
Anyway, different enough and very solid.

Initial rating: 7.2/10

RATING: 7
Fun game, and although important decisions have to be made (what price to sell your tiles for, and which tile to buy from an opponent, and figuring out the optimal placement of the tile), it plays quick enough to get a few games done on one game night.

It is true that while you can never be sure to get the tile(s) you want, some countermeasures can be taken to get profit in as much possible ways as you can.
It may be more profitable to maximize a scroll scoring on items not available on the scoring tiles after all... This may also counter a bit the randomness of the tile drawing.

We also try to convince other players why not to buy your tile, but instead one of the opponents... Having all your tiles bought by opponents leaves you with an awkward feeling you want to avoid.

The only negative point is that from across the table it is nearly impossible to see the details on the tiles of your opponents. But we simply ask if we need more info.

Amazing!
"Kingdom Builder" done right with some borrowed ideas from "Carcassonne".
The auctioning is not everybody's thing, but building a gorgeous little kingdom is great.

in short:

+ good materials and artwork

- a bit of runaway leader problem despite the strong catch-up mechanism
- not much player interaction ?

It might be better to not fully randomize the scoring tiles. Not all work in every place and I'd prefer a good mix of different types (animals/ships, buildings, terrain, tile geometry).

RATING: 6
Solid gameplay. It's a slightly beefed up Carcasonne, but with a player-determined economy bolted on. Instead of simply getting a random tile to add to a communal board, you price and then purchase tiles to add to your individual board. I like how there is natural inflation in the game. I also like how you are forced to purchase tiles you overprice (or tiles you really want and therefore purposefully "overprice" -- sort of a blind auction). In fact, I pretty much like everything about the pricing scheme.

The tile-laying and point-scoring system, however, is merely okay. There's nothing wrong with it, but the shifting score conditions are a bit much in my opinion. You can plan ahead, but it gets fiddly. It feels more like the game is searching for a narrative arc than adding a lot of strategic decisions.

That said, I'd certainly play again.

RATING: 7
I enjoyed Isle of Sky. The problem is, it reminds me very much of 20th Century, another tile-laying game with road connections that generate resources, and I think I like 20th Century more. Isle of Skye is lighter, however, so perhaps it will carve out its own niche. I am interested to use its auctions system more - it raises some fascinating questions about whether you're pricing your tiles to sell or pricing them in hopes of keeping them. Update: Given how quickly this plays in comparison with 20 Century, I do think this has a place in my collection along with its more complex cousin. One quibble grounded on nothing but personal taste: I like the goals/bonus points everyone can achieve far better than the ones that give points only for the first and second place finishers, which can really snowball, cause runaway leaders and skew scores in what is otherwise typically a tight game.

RATING: 6
Reminded me of Carcassonne while the rules were being explained, but it reminded me more of Alhambra merged with a Knizia-esque auction phase while playing.  And with Feld-ish "points salad" scoring.

I liked the auction phase but felt the tile laying phase was way too easy. I'd be surprised if this game has much longevity in gaming circles.  (Though I do like that a lot of the scoring depends on random tile draws, which would at least differentiate each session and prevent the game from being solved.)

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Really like this after one play, and so far this is one of the best game I've played in 2015. There are a couple of things I really like.

First is the scoring tiles. There are 16 scoring tiles, you mix them up and only use four per game. Also, the order in which the tiles are placed makes the game play out differently (different combinations of the four tiles will score at different times during the game). This makes for a ton of replayability and it reminds me of the way scoring goals are handled in Kingdom Builder (although this is a much better game than Kingdom Builder).

The second thing is the price setting mechanic. This game doesn't actually have bidding or auctions in the traditional sense. You put up a screen and secretly set the price for two tiles that you have in front of you. Then everyone removes their screens and starting with the first player each person can buy one tile from an opponent. If no one buys one of your tiles, you get to keep it but you essentially have to pay the price that you set. This makes for an interesting use of money management. You can set a crazy high price for your own tiles if you want to keep them, but then you won't have much money for the next round. And when setting your prices, you lose that money temporarily while waiting for someone to buy one of your tiles. So when setting your prices you want to also try to have enough money leftover in order to buy other tiles. Really interesting idea and it takes a couple of rounds to get the hang of pricing your tiles properly.

The only real issue I can see is that this wouldn't be good for two players (unless there is a two-player variant built in - I played someone else's copy so I'm not familiar with the rulebook). With two players there just wouldn't be enough tiles to be competitive, and it would be entirely possible for no good tiles to come out based on the scoring options for a given game. The game seems like it will be best with 4-5 (I played with 5).

Good game, rating could likely increase after more plays.

[b]Player Count Verdict: 3+[/b]
You need more players just to have more tiles in the game. With two, bad tile draws could result in a pretty uneven game.

RATING: 9.5
Isle of Skye is the surprise hit of 2016 for me. I first played it at SaltCON in March. Before that, I had only seen it mentioned a few times on BGG, and didn't care to try it because I wasn't drawn in by another light tile-laying game. What I always disliked about Carcassonne, was that the driving mechanism in the game was a random tile draw. I felt like there could always have been a better game designed around the way the tiles lay out on the table. So, when I learned the rules to this, I was immediately smitten. The brilliance of Isle of Skye is the way it uses simple rules, but creates a complex, highly-interactive economic system for how players acquire their tiles from round to round. This is a great game for newbies and geeks alike. Highly recommended for both.

RATING: 6.5
Isle of Skye is a quick tile laying game. Every round (there are only six) you get some income, then draw three tiles. One of those tiles you'll discard. The other two you'll price by putting some of your coins next to them indicating the cost. Then, in turn order every player gets to buy a single tile from someone else. They pay you the price you set and you take their coins and the coins you put next to that tile. After everyone has purchased a single tile (or chosen not to), you have to buy any tiles in front of you that weren't bought, by giving up the coins you set next to them. Then everyone places their tiles in their tableau.

Every game will have 4 different random goals that reward for completing certain configurations of tiles, or for various features on those tiles. Not every goal will be scored every round though. Each goal will be scored three times during the game, but the order of scoring varies (which is preset and known at the game outset).

Pricing tiles correctly is hugely important. If you try to price tiles expensively, you may not be able to buy anyone else's tiles... and other people may let you have your expensive tiles, which will bankrupt you for next turn. But price them too low and you'll give away points. You can be screwed and end up with no tiles to place, if you couldn't afford to buy a tile and everyone bought the tiles you had to offer, but you'll be set up for the following round with a lot of cash.

The game is fairly straightforward, and there's nothing truly revolutionary here, but it's quick and fun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
About [thing=176494][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=176494][/thing] is a tile-laying game mixed with buying and selling tiles. During play, people try to increase their regions, in order to best match the varied scoring options. It mixes a bit of strategy and tatical decisions, to form a light game with some long term options.

[thing=176494][/thing] has a fine pacing, as players are only required to give prices to two tiles, choose one to destroy and, afterwards, buy a single tile from someone. The whole process can be quite speditious and downtime is rather low. A complete game, even with the full 5 players, usually lasts for less than 1 hour.

The production value is quite good: the art is pleasant and well done, the screens actually stay up, the tiles are sturdy and the money does its thing. The only complain would be that some tiles have several information and can be difficult to see all of them across a big table - still, is very minor.

Replay value is quite high: the random order of tiles, the different costs and destructions, and more: the different types of scoring and even the very order in which they are resolve, can change, and all of these have deep impact in how the game goes.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
On each turn the player receives three tiles that stays in front of the screen, and behind their screens, players pick one to destroy (return to the bag) and for the other two, they define costs to buy them, using their own money to do so. Once everyone have done this, the costs are revealed. In player order, each person can buy one tile from any other player, paying the cost to the owner, or pass. After each person bought or passed, tiles that weren't purchased are kept by their owners, with the money used to inform the costs being given to the bank. Now each player allocate their tile, following simple placement rules: different terrains on tiles can't be placed together, and roads can end anywhere.

After placement, scoring will happen. The central board informs wich aspects of the regions will be scored (like completed areas, or most boats, or biggest completed sea/lake, etc). A new round will start with player receiving income, and players behind in points, after the third round, will receive more money, as a catch-up mechanism.

At the end of the game, special tiles will score at this point, and for every 5 money the person has, she will gain 1 point.

After 5 rounds of taking tiles, buying and selling, placement and scoring, the person with the most points will be the winner!

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
- Define the cost of tiles. For me, this is the most important decision, not only because it will be what other will have to pay, but also for two reasons: it locks your own money there, meaning you won't be able to use them to buy tiles of others; and because it might net you money (if someone pays) or the tile (for the ones that didn't sell) - both things that are good, however, mainly at the right price;
- Which tile to destroy. The destruction serves two purposes: one, to prevent someone else from getting it; and, two, is possible that, later on, you will be able to buy it from someone else;
- What tile to buy. This is very important, but many times it will be a clear best buy for you. When there isn't, can be relevant to not only look for features in the tile, but also consider both the person that is selling (if you don't buy from someone, that person might not have enough money to buy a tile) and her region (what she will be able to do/score with the tiles if they don't get purchased);
- Where to place a tile. Important, however, normally not all that hard, and is derivative of the costs you put earlier and what you bought. Placing well can mean that, even with smaller regions, you can be able to score better.

As you can see, [thing=176494][/thing] offers a good amount of decisions, many with lasting effects, in a short frame of playing time, being able to be both light enough to not cause a brain burn, at the same time that it is deep enough to please those wanting from more meat in decision-making.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- High replay value;
- Nice production value - is pretty to look at;
- Language independent;
- A strong set of decisions in a small frame of time;
- The region and scoring grow during play, meaning the end is more exciting than the start;
- Not the easiest to explain (considering the different scores, placing rules, etc), but once the game is going, the pacing is fast.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- There is some luck involved in the draw of tiles;
- I found the catch-up mechanism too strong. It is hard to be in a bad place with the buying and selling, as if no one buys your tiles you get them, and if both are, you get the money. Maybe one or the other is better for you, still you don't leave empty handed. Yet the game gives a sizeable amount (specially when playing with 4 or 5 people) of money for those going behind. It isn't game breaking, and for a family game, is understandable to have a catch-up mechanism - still, I was a tad bothered by it;
- There are relevant interaction with the buying and selling, however the game still falls more in the direction of little direct interaction ([thing=822][/thing] , for instance, has more).

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
Like you are in the farmer's market of Carcassonne. You're in the market and want the best deals in order to return home and score better than your neighbours - everyone in their houses wanting to be be king of the street. At a point you will want the best fence painting and topiary; next, for number of chicken eggs and amount of firewood; then chicken eggs and pelts. [thing=176494][/thing] basically is [thing=822][/thing] with money, alongside solitaire regions (everyone have their own) and more things to score, in different moments.

[thing=176494][/thing] hits several good spots: light with decisions, fast with some interaction, building is fun, high on replay while still giving player control or knowledge over things. Their flaws are either minor (the amount of luck), not even considered bad (the level of interaction) or personal (my gripe with the catch-up). Overall, is a winner, with strong positives and weak negatives. It works both for family gaming time as well as in board nights with friends. Recommended.

Often it is used as a long filler or night ender to when we have about 30 minutes left.  Despite its relative ease of play, the bidding mechanic makes it too nuanced for casual gamers...but gives it the staying power for my game group.  People who hate strong catch-up mechanisms will likely be turned off but I believe this is a big reason why it keeps coming back to the table.

RATING: 7
Several comments place this game as a "classic" or "advanced Carcassonne". I find neither of those comments to reflect Isle of Skye. Like Carcassonne, IoS is a tile- laying game where one tries to construct a map of elements which optimizes scoring. While the core auction mechanism itself is not unique, how the mechanism is put together, is. The ability to purchase tiles from opponents, while pricing your own set of tiles with the intention of either selling it for profit or making it prohibitively expensive for others to purchase is a mind-bending exercise. The ability to select and discard a single tile from your individual pool also adds to the complexity of the decision-making tree.

   I consider all these "variables" to be both a strength and weakness of the game.  Some variables can be "adequately predicted" or at least partially "corralled" to help make good decisions. With blind, fixed auctions, that ability is somewhat harder to contain. While you may plan to obtain a certain tile, certain events such as player order or pricing will hamper your carefully laid plans. While you can determine your own pricing of tiles, it is also tough to gauge how opponents may react to your pricing. Also, different players, newbies, veterans or casual gamers often have a different way of gauging "value" of tiles. What one considers reasonable may be too pricey for others. 

   Auctions as a mechanism in gaming can be a wonderful tool to calibrate value of resources in a game. But, for some games, auctions can be an easy way for designers to let players determine their own pricing. It doesn't always work for all games particularly if there are multiple variables intertwined within the auction mechanism. It is much harder to judge value when too many factors, whether subtle or direct, come into play. This is one issue players face in IoS: Which tile to discard? which tiles I want to sell? which tiles do I want to keep? what other tiles people may want? which tiles may be priced out of my reach? What tiles may still be available on my turn? What elements are more valuable for me? Can the tiles be oriented correctly on my map? etc. The variables are numerous and even then, with all the planning, the tile may no longer be available for pickup when your turn comes along.

    Isle of Skye is not a bad game. In fact, I enjoyed it. I find the pace and length of the game to be just right. It's just that I am not sure I can ever find a way to be good at the game. That said, this game requires newbies to play a few games before having a good handle on the value of tiles. With the ever-changing rotation of games and players in some groups, this is can be a shortcoming.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Every player gets a clan screenshot, a "destroy" token and a bit starting money. Then everyone draws 3 tiles which you place in front of your screen.
Secretly everyone now puts the destroy token behind one tile and money behind the other tiles.
Then everyone reveals and the destroyed tiles get put away. Now everyone can buy tiles from other players for the money they put behind the tile. If nothing is bought from you, you can keep them for the price you put behind.
After that, everyone builds their tiles on their clan territory (which only starts with a castle tile) just like in Carcassone. Then the round is evaluated and everyone gets their money and points. The player with the most points is the winner in the end.
This game is phantastic. There are many different tiles that you can purchase and build on but you have to evaluate the right price to not lose it and if not bought to not pay too much for it. It is very cool to put the tiles on your territory in order to win points (the conditions are drawn before the game, there are many different scoring criterias)
However it is best played with "casual" gamers, otherwise they will think a loooooooooong time for their bids.
To me this is one of the best strategy games I've played so far. Clear recommendation

RATING: 5
I played a game of 'Isle of Skye', a tile placement game where you are trying to develop the most advantageous region and 'Why First?' that gets players dumping movement cards on their opponents over five rounds to acquire second place and win the game. Crazy stuff! I'm not normally a fan of tile placement but I did like the mechanism whereby you levy a fee for tiles you draw to either encourage or discourage your opponents from purchasing whilst they do the same to you. I got this slightly wrong in setting my price levels but players can capitalise on the monies they obtain in later rounds so you need to concentrate on which scoring tiles are coming along next so that you can maximise your scoring, whether on ships, each group of four concentric tiles, enclosed areas or animal herds.

RATING: 5
I'm a big fan of games that appear simple at first but reveal more complex dynamics with repeat plays. Reiner Knizia was the master of making 45-90 minute games that fit into this category. Isle of Skye follows the recent trend of doing exactly the opposite- a game that appears more nuanced at first blush than it really is. The pricing system is compelling in theory, but in practice the fact you can only buy one tile means you generally don't want people buying your tiles. Similarly the myriad of scoring tiles suggest a world of possibilities but each play feels more or less the same in terms of the choices you are making.

RATING: 7
This is the first game I've ever seen take a real shot at firing Carcassonne. It's a little heavier, in that it features significantly more features to track and some price setting ala Castles of mad King Ludwig, meaning the learning curve to playing well is a bit higher. In the scheme of things, it is still fairly light so your average gamer will have no problem picking it up.

The goal system is probably the most important addition to the Carcassonne formula, as it gives you tactical direction each turn and helps your evaluations when setting prices for new tiles. This also has the benefit of also giving you a little bit of instant feedback, which again, helps keep your moves on point.

It's surprisingly quick for being a fairly meaty game, or maybe it's fairly meaty for being such a quick game. Either way, it's over in well under an hour and feels just as satisfying as a lot of games that take twice as long.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I’ve seen lots of folks saying Isle of Skye fires Carcassonne for them, and after playing it, I’ll have to respectfully disagree. Yes, this game involves tile laying, but I think the meat of the game is in the buying and selling of the tiles you drew from the bag. Once you have the tiles, you then place them in your kingdom or lands or whatever it’s called and mostly try to line everything up neatly. I say mostly because you can ignore roads if you need to, though it’s better to connect them.

The gist of the game is you’re building your kingdom by placing these tiles. At the end of each round you’ll collect points based upon tiles that are randomly chosen at the beginning of the game. You might get points for having lakes, farms, sheep, money, boats, squares of tiles, and other things. In a given round you’ll get three tiles, secretly price out two of them by placing their money next to them, and select a third to be discarded. Everyone then reveals their tiles and in player order you can buy one tile from another player by paying them the amount of money next to the tile (that player keeps their money and collects yours). If no one buys your tile(s), you get to keep them but lose the money. Whoever has the most points after six rounds wins.

I enjoyed my play of this quite a bit, the meat of it being the pricing which reminded me a lot of Castles of Mad King Ludwig, but with a less interesting theme. On the other hand, it plays quite a bit faster and is quicker to setup. I’ll likely keep it, but I’m not sure for how long.

RATING: N/A
Tile bag builder with auction and bidding. Reminds me of a hybrid of Carcassone, Keyflower, and King Ludwig. This game surprised me at Essen. So fun building your kingdom and so tense hoping you valued your tiles correctly. And the dread when someone buys a tile out from you that you desperately needed. I also love the value consideration of the multiplier scoring aspect from the random objective tiles. Using the axe token is the first game I can recall where a tile is chosen to send back to the bag each round for an added strategy element. Plenty interactive and you have to pay attention to the other kingdoms. Quick pace and managed downtime resulting from a lot of simultaneous effort in planning bids and placing tiles. Even though bid value is limited to two tiles each round it does induce AP moments especially with more players. At first I thought this could be a good travel game but the kingdoms do need table space to grow. All considered, I still love it.

RATING: 7
Very fun game. Variable scoring tiles give it a lot of replay value as well as completely changing the relative value of tiles both game to game and during the course of one game. Paying attention to what is being scored when is vital. Setting prices can be very strategic: do you attempt to get more money or keep the tile with a high bid at the cost of paying for it yourself or make tiles cost less to entice buyers and make a few gold? The artwork is pleasant and components seemed solid as well.

This isn't a Carcassonne killer for me. Carcassonne is static yet tactical, possibly a better 2-3 player game. Isle of Skye changes things up each game and due to the bidding is probably better with 3-5, a player count that I would avoid Carcassonne with naturally. They each inhabit their own niche to me right now.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Beautiful Tile auction game. I would agree that this deserved the Spiel any other year except last year (Legacy should have won). This is the closest a game has made me feel like I am running a market of sorts. I love the simplicity and how different it feels from most games. It is a mish-mash of a lot of other mechanics but basically: Get money, draw three tiles, put one back (ax), auction the other two or pay the amount you set if no one buys them, add them to your own personal Carcassonne and score points depending on what scores in whichever round it is. My only complaint is some of the scoring stipulations can really mess you up if you never seem to get the tiles you need. That aside, this game has the perfect weight of depth, just meaty enough without giving you a headache.

Art- 8/10
Mechanics- 9/10
Theme- 6/10
Fun- 8/10
